The Nuance of al-Muqtadir: Perfect Power without Hindrance
Defines al-Muqtadir and explains the nuance of the افتعل pattern compared to power.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Muqtadir (the All-Able) is distinguished from al-Qadir by indicating perfect, unimpeded power, drawing on the linguistic pattern ifta'ala.

Core Idea
Al-Muqtadir means power that faces no obstacle — a perfect, unhindered ability.

The name al-Muqtadir appears in the Quran and is often translated simply as 'the All-Powerful.' But careful scholars note a deeper nuance: the Arabic pattern ifta'ala (yielding mufta'il) conveys perfection and completeness. In this passage, the author explains this distinction using the analogy of earning (kasaba vs. iktasaba), showing how the same pattern intensifies the meaning. By understanding this, we appreciate the precision of divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic and translation once to get a sense of the argument. Notice the definition of al-Muqtadir and the Quranic citation.
The passage ends abruptly due to a corrupted line.
2
Identify the key distinction
The author mentions that some scholars treat qadara and iqtadara as synonyms, but the investigators differentiate based on the pattern ifta'ala. What does the pattern indicate?
The pattern ifta'ala typically indicates intensity or perfection.
3
Connect to the kasaba/iktasaba analogy
See how the same pattern works in another verb pair: kasaba (earn for self/others) vs. iktasaba (earn solely for self). Reflect on why evil is associated with iktisaba.
This analogy reinforces the idea that ifta'ala adds exclusivity and intensity.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثالث : المقتدر . قال تعالى : ( في مقعد صدق عند مليك مقتدر ) وروزنه مفتعل من القدرة . قال بعضهم : قدر ، واقتدر ، بمعنى واحد . وأما المحققون فقد فرقوا بينهما ، لأن بناء افتعل دلالة على كمال حال القدرة ، فقولنا : اقتدر معناه : أن الفاعل متصرف في فعله كيف شاء وأراد ، لا يعوقه عن ذلك عائق ، ونظيره قوله كسب واكتسب . فإن كسب يستعمل فيها يكسبه الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره . وأما اكتسب فهو مختص بما يفعله الإنسان لنفسه وقال تعالى : ( لها ما كسبت وعليها ما اكتسبت ) وإنما خص الخير بالكسب ، والشر بالاكتساب ، لأن الخير قد يعمله الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره ، فلا جرم لا يكون فيه الجد والرغبة كاملاً ، وأما الشر فلا يعمله إلا لنفسه ، ليلتذبه ، فلا جرم كان الجد فيه أكمل ، فلا جرم خص بلفظ الاكتساب . وأيضاً : أفعال الشريمتنع
The third: al-Muqtadir. He said: 'In a seat of truth in the presence of a Sovereign, Muqtadir.' And its pattern is mufta'il from qudra. Some said: qadara and iqtadara are the same meaning. But the investigators differentiated between them, because the pattern ifta'ala indicates perfection of the state of power. So our saying: iqtadara means that the agent acts in his action as he wills and desires, with no hindrance preventing him. Its analogue is the saying kasaba and iktasaba. For kasaba is used for what one earns for himself and others. But iktasaba is specific to what one does for himself. And He said: 'For her what she earned (kasaba) and against her what she earned (iktasaba).' Indeed, good is specified with kasaba and evil with iktisaba, because good one may do for himself and others, so inevitably the earnestness and desire in it are not complete. As for evil, one does it only for himself to enjoy it, so inevitably the earnestness in it is more complete, so inevitably it is specified with the word iktisaba. And also: the actions of evil are prevented...
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the third name: al-Muqtadir, citing Quran 54:55. The author explains that the word follows the pattern mufta'il from qadara, and notes a disagreement: some say qadara and iqtadara are synonyms, but the investigators differentiate. The pattern ifta'ala (which yields mufta'il) indicates perfection (kamaal) in the state of power. So iqtadara means the agent has complete control to act as he wills without any obstacle. The author illustrates with the pair kasaba and iktasaba: kasaba can be for self or others, while iktasaba is exclusively for oneself, supported by Quran 2:286. Good is associated with kasaba because it may be done for others, so the personal earnestness is less; evil is associated with iktasaba because it is done purely for oneself, so earnestness is greater. The passage ends abruptly with a reference to evil actions being prevented (likely continuing the comparison).

Key Terms
المقتدر
افتعل
اقتدر
كسب
اكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Muqtadir is simply another word for al-Qadir with no difference.
Correction: The word iqtadara (from ifta'ala) indicates perfect, unimpeded power, unlike qadara which may simply mean capability.

Checkpoint
What is the distinction made by the investigators between qadara and iqtadara?
A) They are synonyms.
B) Iqtadara indicates perfection of power and absence of hindrances.
C) Qadara is used only for Allah.
D) There is no distinction.

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Muqtadir emphasizes Allah's perfect power, which is completely unimpeded, as indicated by the intensifying ifta'ala pattern, analogous to the distinction between earning for self and others versus earning solely for self.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You claim Allah is al-Muqtadir, that His power is without hindrance. But are there areas in your life where you act as if something can obstruct Allah's will? What specific situation are you currently holding back from trusting Allah's power because you see an obstacle? Will you today bring that obstacle before Allah and acknowledge that nothing can hinder Him?
?
The analogy of earning for self versus others shows that when we do evil, we do it purely for ourselves with full earnestness. When you sin, do you recognize that your earnestness in sin is a misuse of the drive Allah gave you? How might you channel that same earnestness toward obedience, knowing that Allah's power is perfect and nothing can prevent Him from helping you?
?
Al-Muqtadir means Allah's power is not even potentially hindered. Is there a 'hindrance' in your heart—like despair, pride, or laziness—that you have allowed to limit your reliance on Allah? Will you repent right now and ask Him to remove that hindrance from your heart?

Superiority of Fa'eel Over Fa'il in Divine Names
Author explains the linguistic superiority of fa'eel over fa'il in divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the Arabic pattern fa'eel is used in divine names to emphasize the permanence and strength of Allah's attributes.

Core Idea
The fa'eel pattern in divine names shows that Allah's attributes are permanent and perfect.

This passage explores a subtle linguistic point that has deep theological implications. The author explains that the fa'eel form (like Sami', Rahim) conveys a stronger, more inherent quality than the fa'il form. Understanding this helps us appreciate why the Qur'an prefers certain patterns for divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the examples of fa'il and fa'eel.
2
Identify the two patterns
Find the words 'fa'il' (اسم الفاعل) and 'fa'eel' (بناء الفعيل) in the passage. Note the examples: dakhil vs sami', rahim.
The passage distinguishes between these two forms.
3
Understand the distinction
The author explains that fa'il is used when the action is occasional or effortless, while fa'eel emphasizes permanence and inherent nature.
Think about the theological implications.
4
Examine the Qur'anic evidence
The author cites that 'al-Qadeer' and 'al-'Aleem' occur more often in the Qur'an than 'al-Qadir' and 'al-'Alim', supporting the idea that fa'eel is preferred for divine attributes.
Why would the Qur'an choose a stronger form for Allah?
5
Reflect on the theological significance
Consider how this linguistic point reinforces the belief that Allah's attributes are perfect and unchanging.
This is not just grammar; it's about tawhid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ضعف فيه او قوي . فإنه يجوز أن يشتق منه اسم الفاعل ، كمن يقول : دخل فهو داخل ، وخرج فهو خارج فإذا احتيج إلى تميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر من الفاعل مرة واحدة ، وبين الذي يظهر منه غالباً ، وكذلك إذا احتيج إلى أن يميز بين الفعل الذي يظهر منه على سبيل التكلف وبين الذي يظهر منه على سبيل الخلق والعادة ، وجب العدول إلى هذه الأمثلة ، ليتميز بواسطتها بعض هذه الأقسام عن بعض ومما يدل على أن الفعل أوكد من الفاعل ، أنه يقال سمع فهو سامع ، ورحم فهو راحم . وأما بناء الفعيل فإنه لا يستعمل إلا عند قصد تأكيد الفعل ، لأنا إذا قلنا سميع رحيم دل ذلك على تأكيد معنى الرحمة ، وتمكن هذا الفعل من طباع الموصوف به ، كالخلق الثابت ، وكالطبع اللازم . ولهذا السبب كان لفظ القدير في القرآن أكثر من لفظ القادر ، ولفظ العليم أكثر من العالم .
Weakness in it or strength. For it is permissible to derive the active participle from it, like one who says: entered so he is entering, and exited so he is exiting. So when there is need to distinguish between the action that appears from the agent once and that which appears from it habitually, and likewise when there is need to distinguish between the action that appears from it with effort and that which appears from it by nature and custom, it is necessary to turn to these patterns, so that some of these categories are distinguished from others by means of them. And among what indicates that the action is stronger than the agent is that it is said: he heard so he is hearing, and he had mercy so he is merciful. As for the construction of fa'eel, it is not used except when intending to emphasize the action, because if we say 'Sami'un Rahimun' that indicates emphasizing the meaning of mercy and the firm establishment of this action in the nature of the described, like a fixed character and an inherent disposition. For this reason the word 'al-Qadeer' occurs in the Qur'an more than the word 'al-Qadir', and the word 'al-'Aleem' more than 'al-'Alim'.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage is a linguistic and theological analysis of the patterns fa'il and fa'eel as they apply to divine names. The author begins by stating the general principle that when there is a need to distinguish between an action that occurs once versus habitually, or with effort versus naturally, one must use different derived forms. He then notes that the action noun (fa'l) is stronger than the active participle (fa'il), citing the examples 'sami'a' (he heard) giving 'sami'' (hearing) and 'rahima' (he had mercy) giving 'rahim' (merciful). The pattern fa'eel, he explains, is used specifically to emphasize the action and indicate its firm establishment in the nature of the described, like a fixed character. He concludes that this linguistic principle explains why the Qur'an uses al-Qadeer more often than al-Qadir, and al-'Aleem more than al-'Alim, since the fa'eel form conveys a stronger, more permanent attribute.

Key Terms
اسم الفاعل
بناء الفعيل
تأكيد الفعل
القدير
القادر
العليم
العالم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think this is merely a grammatical observation with no theological importance.
Correction: This distinction is crucial because it reinforces the permanence and perfection of Allah's attributes, a key tenet of Islamic theology.

Checkpoint
Why does the author say the Qur'an uses 'al-Qadeer' more often than 'al-Qadir'?
A) Because 'al-Qadeer' is shorter and easier to pronounce.
B) Because the pattern fa'eel emphasizes the strength and permanence of the attribute.
C) Because 'al-Qadir' is not an authentic divine name.
D) Because the Qur'an always prefers longer words for majesty.

Key Takeaway
The fa'eel pattern in divine names emphasizes the permanence and perfection of Allah's attributes, which is why the Qur'an often prefers it.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah as al-Qadeer, do you truly feel that His power is constant and unchanging, or do you only think of His power in moments of need? How would your reliance on Him change if you internalized the fa'eel pattern?
?
The fa'eel pattern indicates that mercy (rahima) becomes like a fixed character in the described. Do you believe that Allah's mercy is as inherent to Him as your own character traits are to you? In what area of your life do you need to remind yourself of this permanent mercy?
?
The Qur'an uses 'al-'Aleem' more than 'al-'Alim'. Do you live as if Allah knows everything permanently, or do you sometimes act as if He only knows what you show? What secret habit would change if you truly believed in al-'Aleem?

The Divine Name Al-Muqtadir and Its Nuance
Author defines al-muqtadir and distinguishes it from al-qadir using the kasb analogy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between al-Qadir and al-Muqtadir, and why al-Muqtadir implies perfect power without impediment.

Core Idea
Al-Muqtadir means perfect power without obstacles, like acquiring good deeds for oneself.

This passage from al-Matalib al-Aliyah explores a subtle distinction within the divine attribute of power. The author explains that while al-Qadir denotes ability, al-Muqtadir (from the pattern ifta'ala) indicates a perfection of that ability — acting without any hindrance. To clarify, he draws an analogy with the Arabic verbs kasaba and iktasaba, showing how the extra letters intensify the meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without over-analyzing, then the translation. Let the author's flow come through.
Pay attention to the key terms: al-Muqtadir, kasaba, iktasaba.
2
Identify the two claims
Note that some say qadara and iqtadara are synonymous, but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish them. This distinction is central.
3
Understand the kasb analogy
The author uses kasaba (earn) and iktasaba (acquire) to illustrate. Notice how iktasaba implies personal effort and exclusivity.
The verse 'To her what she has earned…' is key.
4
Apply the analogy to good and evil
Good deeds are called kasb because they can be done for others; evil is iktisab because it is done solely for oneself. This shows greater resolve for evil.
The author extends this to religious obligations, which require even greater effort.
5
Reflect on theological significance
Consider how this nuance enhances your understanding of Allah's power: it is not just ability, but perfect, unimpeded power.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
لفظ الثالث : المقتدر . قال تعالى : ( في مقعد صدق عند مليك مقتدر ) ووزنه مفتعل من القدرة . قال بعضهم : قدر، واقتدر، بمعنى واحد . وأما المحققون فقد فرقوا بينهما، لأن بناء افتعل دلالة على كمال حال القدرة، فقولنا : اقتدر معناه : أن الفاعل متصرف في فعله كيف شاء وأراد، لا يعوقه عن ذلك عائق، ونظيره قوله كسب واكتسب . فإن كسب يستعمل فيما يكتسبه الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره . وأما اكتسب فهو مختص بما يفعله الإنسان لنفسه قال تعالى : ( لها ما كسبت وعليها ما اكتسبت ) وإنما خص الخير بالكسب، والشر بالاكتساب، لأن الخير قد يعمله الإنسان لنفسه ولغيره، فلا جرم لا يكون فيه الجد والرغبة كاملاً، وأما الشر فلا يعمله إلا لنفسه، ليلتذ به، فلا جرم كان الجد فيه أكمل، فلا جرم خص بلفظ الاكتساب . وأيضاً : أفعال الشريعة تمنع عنها بالزواجر الشرعية . فلا بد من الجد في إدخالها في الوجود، وإلا لم تحصل أفعال الخير لأن الموانع عنها دائمة . والله أعلم
The third term: al-Muqtadir. He said, the Exalted: 'In a seat of truth near a Sovereign, Omnipotent' and its pattern is muf'tal from power. Some said: qadara and iqtadara are the same meaning. But the verifiers have distinguished between them, because the construction of ifta'ala indicates the perfection of the state of power. So our saying 'iqtadara' means that the agent acts in his action as he wills and desires, no obstacle hindering him from that. Its analogue is the saying 'kasaba' and 'iktasaba'. For 'kasaba' is used for what a person earns for himself and others. As for 'iktasaba', it is specific to what a person does for himself. He said, the Exalted: 'To her what she has earned and against her what she has acquired.' And the reason good deeds are specified with 'kasb' and evil with 'iktisab' is that good deeds a person may do for himself and others; consequently the exertion and desire in it are not complete. As for evil, he only does it for himself to enjoy it; consequently the exertion in it is more complete, so it is specifically designated with the word 'iktisab'. Also, the actions of the Law are prevented by legal deterrents, so exertion is necessary to bring them into existence, otherwise acts of good would not occur because the obstacles are constant. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 272

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Muqtadir and explains its nuance. The author begins by citing the Qur'anic verse 'In a seat of truth near a Sovereign, Omnipotent' (al-Qamar 54:55) and notes that the word follows the pattern muf'tal from qudra (power). He mentions that some consider qadara and iqtadara synonymous, but the verifiers (muhaqqiqun) distinguish them: the form ifta'ala indicates perfect power, meaning the agent acts without any hindrance. To illustrate, he compares kasaba (to earn) and iktasaba (to acquire), showing that iktasaba implies personal effort and exclusivity, as in the verse 'To her what she has earned and against her what she has acquired' (al-Baqarah 2:286). He explains that good deeds are called kasb because they can be done for others, while evil is called iktisab because it is done solely for oneself, requiring greater resolve. He extends this to religious obligations, which require even greater effort because of legal deterrents.

Key Terms
المقتدر
القدرة
افتعل
كسب
اكتسب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Muqtadir is synonymous with al-Qadir.
Correction: The verifiers distinguish them: al-Muqtadir implies perfect power without obstacle, as shown by the ifta'ala pattern.

Checkpoint
According to the verifiers, what distinguishes iqtadara from qadara?
A) They are completely synonymous.
B) Iqtadara indicates power that is hindered.
C) Iqtadara indicates perfect power without any obstacle.
D) Qadara is used only for divine action.

Key Takeaway
Al-Muqtadir reveals that Allah's power is absolute and unimpeded, a perfection beyond mere ability.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If Allah’s power is absolute and unimpeded, what obstacle in your life have you been exaggerating as if it could stop His will? Will you today replace that lie with trust in al-Muqtadir?
?
The author notes that evil deeds are done with intense resolve because they are solely for oneself. When have you found yourself more determined to sin than to obey? What does that reveal about the state of your heart’s desires, and what will you do to redirect that intensity toward worship?
?
You have learned that al-Muqtadir means no barrier can hinder Allah. Yet you often allow fear, doubt, or worldly concerns to paralyze you from acting upon His commands. In which specific area of obedience will you step forward this week, fully believing that His power makes all obstacles irrelevant for you?

The Divine Names Al-Qawi and Al-Matin
Author defines al-qawi and al-matin, distinguishing active and passive strength.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meanings of the divine names al-Qawi (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm), and how they relate to active power and impassibility.

Core Idea
Allah's strength encompasses both perfect action and perfect impassibility.

In this passage, the author clarifies two complementary names: al-Qawi, which emphasizes Allah's active power to effect, and al-Matin, which emphasizes His immunity from being affected. This distinction is rooted in a linguistic analysis of strength and firmness, and it culminates in a theological conclusion grounded in Allah's necessary existence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and note the two definitions of strength
Read the Arabic passage and translation. The author first gives two everyday meanings of 'strong': one who can effect things, and something that resists being affected, like a stone. Identify these two senses.
These two meanings are the key to understanding the two divine names.
2
Understand the linguistic derivation of 'Matin'
The author explains that 'matin' (firmness) is derived from 'matn' (the back), because the back gives stability. This shows that al-Matin implies an unyielding firmness that cannot be overcome.
The linguistic root reinforces the idea of impassibility.
3
Apply the two meanings to Allah
The author now assigns the first meaning (active power) to al-Qawi, and the second meaning (impassibility) to al-Matin. He then explains why Allah must be al-Matin: because He is necessary of existence, He cannot be affected by anything else.
This connects the attributes directly to tawhid and the concept of wajib al-wujud.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع والخامس : القوي المتين. واعلم أن القادر على الشيء المؤثر فيه يسمى قوياً عليه. وأيضاً : الشيء الذي لا يتأثر عن غيره يسمى قوياً كما يقال : هذا الحجر قوي . فالحاصل : أن القوة الفاعلة تسمى قوة، والإستعداد الشديد لعدم الانفعال يسمى أيضاً قوة، وقد يسمى أيضاً : متانة . ومعنى المثانة : الصلابة، وهي مأخوذة من المتن، الذي هو الظهر، لأن استمساك أكثر الحيوان يكون بالظهر . فالقوة في حق الله تعالى . عبارة عن كونه قادراً على التأثير. والمتين في حقه عبارة عن امتناع تأثره عن غيره، وذلك لأنه واجب الوجود لذاته؛ في ذاته، وفي جميع صفاته، فيمتنع تأثره عن الغير والله أعلم
The fourth and fifth names: al-Qawi and al-Matin. Know that the one who is capable over a thing and effects it is called strong over it. Also, a thing that is not affected by others is called strong, as is said: this stone is strong. So the conclusion: that the active power is called strength, and the intense readiness for non-affectedness is also called strength, and it is also called firmness. The meaning of 'matan' is hardness, derived from 'matn', which is the back, because the stability of most animals is through the back. So 'al-quwwa' with respect to Allah, the Exalted, is an expression of His being capable of effecting. And 'al-matin' with respect to Him is an expression of the prevention of His being affected by others, and that is because He is necessary of existence in Himself, in His essence, and in all His attributes; so His being affected by others is impossible. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names: al-Qawi (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). The author begins by giving two meanings of 'strong': one who has power to effect an action, and something that is not affected by others, like a strong stone. He summarizes that strength includes both active power (al-quwwa al-fa'ila) and intense readiness to resist being affected, which is also called firmness (matana). He derives 'matan' from the back (al-matin), since animals rely on the back for stability. Applying this to Allah, 'al-Qawi' means He is capable of effecting, while 'al-Matin' means He is immune to being affected by others. This immunity is a consequence of His being necessary of existence (wajib al-wujud) in His essence and all attributes.

Key Terms
القوي
المتين
القوة الفاعلة
الاستعداد لعدم الانفعال
واجب الوجود

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'strong' implies a need to exert effort or struggle, or that Allah's strength is like human strength.
Correction: The author clarifies that Allah's strength is perfect: it includes both the power to effect without any exertion and the absolute inability to be affected by anything else, due to His necessary existence.

Checkpoint
Why is Allah described as al-Matin?
A) Because He has great physical strength.
B) Because He is firm in His promises.
C) Because He is impossible to be affected by others due to His necessary existence.
D) Because He supports His creation like a back.

Key Takeaway
Al-Qawi signifies Allah's active power, while al-Matin signifies His impassibility, both grounded in His necessary existence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you feel weak and vulnerable, do you truly take refuge in Allah's al-Qawi and al-Matin, or do you rely on your own strength? What would change in your daily life if you fully internalized that Allah's strength is both active and unshakeable?
?
Consider a time you were deeply affected by someone's criticism or a difficult circumstance. How does knowing that Allah is al-Matin—utterly unaffected and impassible—reshape your perspective on that experience? Are you seeking His firmness or are you still shaken by what others think or do?
?
If Allah's al-Qawi means He perfectly effects everything, and His al-Matin means nothing affects Him, then what is the only proper response of your heart and actions? Will you commit today to surrendering your need to control outcomes and instead trust in His complete power and unassailable nature?

The Fifth Category: Names Indicating Divine Knowledge
Author enumerates three names from the category of divine knowledge.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand three key names from the fifth category of divine names that relate to Allah's attribute of knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah's knowledge is affirmed through names like al-'Aleem and al-'Allam.

In this lesson, we begin exploring the fifth category of divine names: those that indicate knowledge. The author introduces several words that fall under this category, emphasizing that they are numerous. He then provides three foundational examples: the affirmation of knowledge itself, the name al-'Aleem (the All-Knowing), and al-'Allam (the Knower of all unseen things). Each is supported by Qur'anic verses.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyze it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm and the Qur'anic verses sink in.
Notice that the author says 'these words are many' before listing only three examples.
2
Identify the three names
The passage lists three items under the fifth category. Find each one: إثبات العلم, العالم, العلام. Note the supporting verses for each.
Each name is introduced with 'One of them:' / 'The second:' / 'The third:'.
3
Reflect on the category's significance
Ask yourself why knowledge is given its own category among divine names. The commentary will help explain that knowing these names deepens understanding of Allah's omniscience.
This connects to the broader theme of divine attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من أسماء الله تعالى : الأسماء الدالة على العلم :
وهذه الألفاظ كثيرة :
أحدها : إثبات العلم الله تعالى. قال تعالى : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) وقال : ( ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه ) وقال : ( أنزله بعلمه ) وقال : ( ولا تضع إلا بعلمه ) وقال : ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل بعلم الله )
وثانيها : العالم . قال تعالى : ( هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة )
وثالثها : العلام . قال تعالى : ( إنك أنت علام الغيوب )
The fifth category of the names of Allah, the Exalted: the names indicating knowledge. And these words are many: One of them: the affirmation of knowledge for Allah, the Exalted. He said, the Exalted: 'Indeed, Allah - with Him is the knowledge of the Hour.' And He said: 'And they encompass not of His knowledge.' And He said: 'He sent it down with His knowledge.' And He said: 'And no female conceives except with His knowledge.' And He said: 'So know that what was sent down is with the knowledge of Allah.' The second: al-'Aleem. He said, the Exalted: 'He is Allah, other than whom there is no deity, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed.' The third: al-'Allam. He said, the Exalted: 'Indeed, You are the All-Knower of the unseen.'
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage begins the fifth category of divine names: those related to knowledge. The author states that these expressions are many, then lists three foundational ones. First is 'ithbat al-'ilm' (affirmation of knowledge), citing Qur'anic verses affirming God's knowledge of the Hour, His encompassing knowledge, sending down revelation with His knowledge, and His knowledge of all creation. Second is 'al-'Aleem' (the All-Knowing), with the verse from al-Hashr 59:22. Third is 'al-'Allam' (the All-Knower of the unseen), from al-Ma'idah 5:116? Actually it's from al-Ma'ida? The verse is 'You are the All-Knower of the unseen' from Surah al-Ma'ida 5:116 or Surah al-Hashr? Correctly, 'Innaka anta 'Allamu al-ghuyub' appears in Surah al-Ma'ida 5:116. This passage is introductory, listing the first names in this category.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Allah says, 'Nor does a female conceive except with His knowledge.' He knows every private moment, every thought you hide, every secret sin. If you truly believed that, would you still do the things you do when no one is watching? What is one hidden habit you must bring into the light of His awareness today?
?
The passage affirms that Allah is 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed.' He sees your prayers and your neglect, your charity and your stinginess. Does your daily life reflect a genuine awareness that you are always before the All-Knowing? What would change if you lived every moment conscious of His sight?
?
Al-'Aleem and Al-'Allam are among His beautiful names. How often do you address Him by these names in your du'a? Are you comfortable knowing that nothing you ask—no matter how small or hidden—escapes His knowledge? Commit to calling upon Him as Al-'Aleem this week, asking Him for guidance in an area where you feel lost.

The Names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin: Divine Power and Impassibility
Defines the divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin, distinguishing active power from impassibility.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between active power and impassibility in the divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin, and why both are necessary for affirming God's transcendence.

Core Idea
God's strength is active power; His firmness is utter impassibility.

This passage explores two closely related divine names: al-Qawiyy (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). The author first clarifies the linguistic range of 'strength'—it can mean either the ability to affect or the inability to be affected. By applying both meanings to God, we affirm that He is both all‑powerful over creation and completely beyond being influenced by it. This safeguards His transcendence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the two distinct meanings of 'strong' that the author presents.
Pay attention to the examples: the agent who effects change vs. the stone that resists change.
2
Trace the derivation of matāna
The author explains that the second sense of strength is also called matāna, derived from matn (back). Reflect on why the back is used as a metaphor for firmness.
3
Apply the names to God
Understand how each name applies: al-Qawiyy means He can effect, al-Matin means He cannot be affected. Link this to His being the Necessary Existent.
The commentary will clarify the theological implications.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع والخامس : القوي المتين. واعلم أن القادر على الشيء المؤثر فيه يسمى قوياً عليه. وأيضاً : الشيء الذي لا يتأثر عن غيره يسمى ( قوياً ) كما يقال : هذا الحجر قوي . فالحاصل : أن القوة الفاعلة تسمى قوة ، والإستعداد الشديد لعدم الانفعال يسمى أيضاً قوة ، وقد يسمى أيضاً : متانة . ومعنى المثانة : الصلابة ، وهي مأخوذة من المتن ، الذي هو الظهر ، لأن استمساك أكثر الحيوان يكون بالظهر . فالقوة في حق الله تعالى . عبارة عن كونه قادراً على التأثير. والمتين في حقه عبارة عن امتناع تأثره عن غيره ، وذلك لأنه واجب الوجود لذاته ؛ في ذاته ، وفي جميع صفاته ، فيمتنع تأثره عن الغير ( والله أعلم )
The fourth and fifth names: al-Qawiyy (the Strong) and al-Matin (the Firm). Know that the one who is able to do something and effects it is called strong (qawiyy) with respect to it. Also: a thing that is not affected by others is called strong (qawiyy), as is said: 'This stone is strong.' So the conclusion: the active power is called quwwa (strength), and the intense readiness to not being affected is also called quwwa, and it may also be called matāna (firmness). The meaning of matāna is hardness, and it is derived from matn (back), because the stability of most animals is through the back. So quwwa with respect to God Almighty is an expression of His being able to effect. And al-Matin with respect to Him is an expression of the impossibility of His being affected by others, because He is Necessary Existence by Himself; in His essence and in all His attributes, it is impossible for Him to be affected by others. And God knows best.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage defines two divine names: al-Qawiyy and al-Matin. The author begins by distinguishing two linguistic uses of 'strong' (qawiyy): (1) one who has power over something and affects it, and (2) something that resists being affected by others, like a hard stone. From this, he derives that 'quwwa' can mean either active power or passive firmness, the latter also called 'matāna'. He then explains that 'matāna' comes from 'matn' (back), alluding to how animals derive stability from their backs. Applying this to God, 'quwwa' means He is powerful to effect, while 'al-Matin' means He is impossible to be affected by anything else, because He is the Necessary Existent in His essence and all His attributes. This safeguards divine transcendence against any notion of passivity.

Key Terms
القوي
المتين
القوة
المتانة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That God's strength is like physical strength or that being 'firm' implies physical solidity.
Correction: The passage explains that al-Qawiyy means He has power to effect, and al-Matin means He cannot be affected—neither implies physicality.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does 'al-Matin' mean when applied to God?
A) That He is physically solid like a stone
B) That He is able to do everything
C) That He cannot be affected by anything else
D) That He has a strong back

Key Takeaway
The divine names al-Qawiyy and al-Matin together affirm that God is both all‑powerful over creation and completely impervious to being affected by it, because He is the Necessary Existent.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that God is absolutely impassible – He cannot be affected by anything. What does this mean for your habits of speech when you pray? Do you sometimes talk to God as if He needs your praise or as if your words change His attitude toward you? How should your prayers change, knowing that God is utterly self-sufficient and unaffected?
?
If God's power is not like physical strength, how do you instinctively imagine God's control over the universe? Have you ever thought of God as 'trying hard' or 'struggling' to manage creation? How does the distinction between active power and impassibility correct that mental picture, and what does that demand of your trust in Him?
?
The source says God cannot be affected in His essence or attributes. That means your sin does not 'hurt' God or cause Him to 'react' emotionally. But you still need to repent. Why? If God is unchanged by your disobedience, what is the real purpose of your turning back to Him? Let that question pierce your excuses and lead to genuine repentance today.

Divine Names Indicating Knowledge: Permissible and Impermissible Forms
Enumerates seven words for God's knowledge with rulings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which Arabic words for knowledge can be used as divine names and why some forms are impermissible.

Core Idea
Not every 'knowledge' word fits Allah; linguistic nuance guides divine naming.

This passage from al-Matālib al-ʿĀliyah systematically lists seven words related to knowledge found in the Qur'an or Arabic usage. For each, the author provides a Qur'anic verse and then rules on whether it may be used as a name of Allah. This careful selection shows the importance of linguistic precision in theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the entire passage
Read the Arabic passage once without stopping. Then read the translation. Notice the pattern: each item is introduced by a number, followed by a verse, and sometimes a ruling.
The passage is a numbered list; pay attention to the transitions.
2
Identify the seven words
List the seven words: العلم (affirming knowledge), العالم, العلام, الأعلم, العلم (as noun), العليم, العلامة. Note which ones are permissible and which are not.
3
Understand the rulings
For each word, check the author's reason for permissibility or impermissibility. The key is that some forms imply deficiency (العلم as a noun) or acquisition through effort (العلامة).
4
Apply the principle
Ask yourself: why is العليم permissible but العلامة not? The difference lies in the form's connotations. This teaches that divine names must be free from any hint of imperfection.
This connects to the broader rule of tawqif in divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
القسم الخامس من أسماء الله تعالى : الأسماء الدالة على العلم : وهذه الألفاظ كثيرة : أحدها : إثبات العلم الله تعالى. قال تعالى : ( إن الله عنده علم الساعة ) وقال : ( ولا يحيطون بشيء من علمه ) وقال : ( أنزله بعلمه ) وقال : ( ولا تضع إلا بعلمه ) وقال : ( فاعلموا أنما أنزل يعلم الله ) وثانيها : العالم . قال تعالى : ( هو الله الذي لا إله إلا هو عالم الغيب والشهادة ) وثالثها : العلام . قال تعالى : ( إنك أنت علام الغيوب ) ورابعها : الأعلم . قال تعالى : ( ربكم أعلم بكم ) وخامسها : العلم . قال تعالى : ( الرحمن علم القرآن ) و ( علمك ما لم تكن تعلم ) إلا أن هذا اللفظ لا يذكر في حق الله تعالى ، لأنه لفظ يوهم نوعاً من الدناءة . وسادسها : العليم . قال تعالى : ( وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به . إنه عليم بذات الصدور ) وسابعها : العلامة . يقال : رجل علامة إذا وصف بكثرة العلم ، ولا يجوز استعماله في حق الله تعالى . لأن هذه اللفظة وضعت لمن خرج عن القلة والنقصان إلى الكثرة والكمال ، بسبب التكلف والارتباض . وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال .
The fifth section of the names of God Almighty: the names indicating knowledge. These words are many: First: affirming knowledge ('ilm) for God Almighty. He said: 'Indeed, Allah [alone] has knowledge of the Hour' (31:34) and 'they encompass not a thing of His knowledge' (2:255) and 'He sent it down with His knowledge' (4:166) and 'no female conceives except with His knowledge' (35:11) and 'know that what is revealed is from Allah' (11:14). Second: al-'Ālim (the Knower). He said: 'He is Allah, other than whom there is no deity, Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (59:22). Third: al-'Allām (the All-Knowing). He said: 'Indeed, You are the All-Knowing of the unseen' (5:116). Fourth: al-A'lam (the Most Knowing). He said: 'Your Lord is most knowing of you' (17:54). Fifth: al-'Ilm (Knowledge). He said: 'The Most Merciful taught the Qur'an' (55:1-2) and 'He taught you that which you did not know' (4:113), but this word is not mentioned with respect to God Almighty, because it suggests a degree of lowliness. Sixth: al-'Alīm (the All-Knowing). He said: 'And conceal your speech or publicize it; indeed, He is Knowing of that within the breasts' (67:13). Seventh: al-'Allāma (the very learned). It is said: a man is 'allāma when described with much knowledge, but its use for God Almighty is not permissible, because this word was coined for one who emerges from paucity and deficiency to abundance and perfection through effort and training. That is impossible with respect to God Almighty.
Source pages p. 273

Commentary
This passage categorizes divine names that express knowledge. The author lists seven words that appear in the Qur'an or Arabic usage. For each, he provides a Qur'anic proof. He then rules on permissibility: (1) 'Ilm (knowledge itself) is affirmed but the verbal noun form is not used directly for God because it can imply a deficiency (as if knowledge comes and goes). (2) 'Ālim, 'Allām, A'lam, 'Alīm are all permissible. (3) 'Allāma (intensive form) is not permissible because it normally describes a human who acquires knowledge through effort, which cannot apply to God. This shows careful sensitivity to linguistic nuance in divine naming.

Key Terms
العالم
العلام
الأعلم
العليم
العلامة
العلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any Arabic word meaning 'knowing' can be used as a name of Allah.
Correction: The passage shows that some forms like العلامة and العلم (verbal noun) are impermissible due to their connotations of deficiency or acquisition.

Checkpoint
Why is the word 'al-ʿAllāma' not used for Allah?
A) It is not found in the Qur'an
B) It implies acquiring knowledge through effort
C) It means 'the most knowing' and only Allah knows the unseen
D) It indicates a deficiency in power

Key Takeaway
Divine names must be free from any connotation of imperfection; not every knowledge-related word is suitable for Allah.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you call upon Allah using His names, how often do you pause to consider whether the name you use truly reflects His perfection without any hint of deficiency? What might change if you were more deliberate about this?
?
The passage teaches that even a superlative form like 'allāma is unsuitable for Allah because it implies effort and acquisition. Is there any attribute you have unknowingly ascribed to Allah that carries a hidden trace of createdness? How will you correct that starting today?
?
If the names of Allah must be free from any taint of imperfection, what does that demand of your own speech when you describe Allah in your du'a or daily conversations? Commit to one specific name from this lesson that you will use more consciously this week.

The First Meaning of al-Latif: Rejecting Physical Smallness
Explains rejection of physical smallness and affirmation of tanzih.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the literal meaning of 'smallness' cannot apply to Allah and how it is reinterpreted as an attribute of transcendence (tanzih).

Core Idea
Allah is not small; He is beyond sensory perception.

This lesson examines the first meaning of the divine name 'al-Latif'. The author begins by stating a literal meaning that is impossible for Allah, then reinterprets it as a negative attribute. We'll see how this establishes the principle of tanzih, which is foundational in Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then read the translation. Notice how the author first presents the literal meaning, then rejects it, and finally offers an alternative interpretation.
The verse at the beginning provides context for the name.
2
Identify the rejected meaning
Find where the author says the literal meaning is impossible for Allah. Understand why physical smallness cannot be attributed to the Creator.
3
Understand the reinterpretation
The author replaces the rejected meaning with a necessary consequence: that the senses cannot perceive Allah. This is an attribute of tanzih (transcendence). Reflect on how this shifts the meaning from a physical to a metaphysical quality.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : اللطيف . قال تعالى : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ؟ وهو اللطيف الخبير ) واعلم أن « اللطيف » له معاني . فالأول : أن الشيء الصغير جداً ، الذي لا يحس به لغاية صغره يسمى لطيفاً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . فوجب حمله على لازمة ، وهو أن الحواس لا تقف عليه لكونه لطيفاً ، وبهذا التفسير يكون من صفات التنزيه . الثاني : اللطيف هو العالم بدقائق الأمور وغوامضها ، يقال فلان لطيف اليد. إذا كان حاذقاً في صنعته ، مهتدياً إلى ما يشكل على غيره ، وعلى هذا التقدير فكونه لطيفاً عبارة عن علمه . والثالث : اللطيف هو البر بعباده الذي يلطف بهم من حيث لا يعلمون ، وي هيء مصالحهم من حيث إنهم لا يحتسبون ، ومنه قوله تعالى : الله لطيف بعباده ) والرابع : قال الغزالي - رحمه الله - : وهذا الاسم ...
The second name: al-Latif (the Subtle). He said: 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Acquainted?' (67:14). Know that 'al-Latif' has meanings. First: that something very small, which is not perceived due to its extreme smallness, is called latif. This is impossible with respect to God Almighty. So it must be taken as its necessary consequence, namely that the senses do not perceive Him because He is subtle (latif), and by this interpretation it is among the attributes of transcendence. Second: al-Latif is the one who knows the fine points of matters and their intricacies. It is said: 'so-and-so is latif al-yad (lit. subtle of hand)' if he is skillful in his craft, guided to what is difficult for others. On this basis, His being latif is an expression of His knowledge. Third: al-Latif is the One who is kind (barr) to His servants, who is gentle (yaltuf) with them in ways they do not know, and arranges their interests in ways they do not expect; from this is His saying: 'Allah is kind to His servants' (42:19). Fourth: al-Ghazali – may God have mercy on him – said: 'And this name...'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name 'al-Latif'. The author gives three distinct meanings, plus a fourth from al-Ghazali that is cut off. The first meaning is the literal sense of physical smallness, which is impossible for God; instead it is interpreted as 'the senses cannot perceive Him' – a negative attribute (tanzih). The second meaning is 'the one who knows the finest details of things', using the analogy of a craftsman with a 'subtle hand' – this makes al-Latif an attribute of knowledge. The third meaning is 'the One who is kind and gentle to His servants', providing for them in unexpected ways, supported by Qur'an 42:19. The fourth meaning from al-Ghazali begins but is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
اللطيف
التنزيه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think 'al-Latif' means that Allah is physically small or subtle in a material sense.
Correction: The passage explicitly rejects that and explains that it means Allah is beyond sensory perception, an attribute of transcendence.

Checkpoint
What does the author do with the first meaning of 'al-Latif'?
A) Accepts it literally for Allah
B) Rejects it and reinterprets it as transcendence
C) Ignores it entirely
D) Claims it applies only to prophets

Key Takeaway
The first meaning of al-Latif is not about size but about Allah's transcendence beyond sensory perception.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever caught yourself imagining Allah as 'subtle' in a way that reduces His majesty—like a gentle breeze or a fine mist? How does this lesson challenge that mental image and call you to a higher tanzih?
?
If your senses cannot perceive Allah, then what does it mean to 'feel' His presence? In what concrete ways can you train yourself to seek Him beyond sensory experience, starting today?
?
This passage shows that a word (latif) can be misunderstood and must be corrected to protect Allah's transcendence. Is there an attribute of Allah you have been describing in a human-like way without realizing it? Write down one and commit to studying its correct meaning from the Qur'an and Sunnah.

Al-Latif as Knowledge and Kindness
Explains second and third meanings of al-Latif as knowledge and kindness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second and third meanings of al-Latif, which affirm Allah's knowledge of subtle details and His gentle kindness to creation.

Core Idea
Al-Latif reveals Allah's perfect knowledge and gentle care.

The second and third meanings of 'al-Latif' shift from negation to affirmation. The author presents two positive attributes: comprehensive knowledge of all subtleties, and boundless kindness toward His servants. We'll explore these meanings with Quranic support and see how they complement the first meaning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic and translation for the second and third meanings. Note the examples and Quranic verse provided.
2
Analyze the second meaning
The author defines al-Latif as the one who knows the fine points of all things. He uses the analogy of a skilled craftsman. Understand how this relates to divine knowledge.
3
Analyze the third meaning
The author defines al-Latif as kind and gentle, arranging servants' affairs without their awareness. Note the Quranic evidence.
The third meaning emphasizes Allah's benevolence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : اللطيف . قال تعالى : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ؟ وهو اللطيف الخبير ) واعلم أن « اللطيف » له معاني . فالأول : أن الشيء الصغير جداً ، الذي لا يحس به لغاية صغره يسمى لطيفاً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . فوجب حمله على لازمة ، وهو أن الحواس لا تقف عليه لكونه لطيفاً ، وبهذا التفسير يكون من صفات التنزيه . الثاني : اللطيف هو العالم بدقائق الأمور وغوامضها ، يقال فلان لطيف اليد. إذا كان حاذقاً في صنعته ، مهتدياً إلى ما يشكل على غيره ، وعلى هذا التقدير فكونه لطيفاً عبارة عن علمه . والثالث : اللطيف هو البر بعباده الذي يلطف بهم من حيث لا يعلمون ، وي هيء مصالحهم من حيث إنهم لا يحتسبون ، ومنه قوله تعالى : الله لطيف بعباده ) والرابع : قال الغزالي - رحمه الله - : وهذا الاسم ...
The second name: al-Latif (the Subtle). He said: 'Does He who created not know, while He is the Subtle, the Acquainted?' (67:14). Know that 'al-Latif' has meanings. First: that something very small, which is not perceived due to its extreme smallness, is called latif. This is impossible with respect to God Almighty. So it must be taken as its necessary consequence, namely that the senses do not perceive Him because He is subtle (latif), and by this interpretation it is among the attributes of transcendence. Second: al-Latif is the one who knows the fine points of matters and their intricacies. It is said: 'so-and-so is latif al-yad (lit. subtle of hand)' if he is skillful in his craft, guided to what is difficult for others. On this basis, His being latif is an expression of His knowledge. Third: al-Latif is the One who is kind (barr) to His servants, who is gentle (yaltuf) with them in ways they do not know, and arranges their interests in ways they do not expect; from this is His saying: 'Allah is kind to His servants' (42:19). Fourth: al-Ghazali – may God have mercy on him – said: 'And this name...'
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name 'al-Latif'. The author gives three distinct meanings, plus a fourth from al-Ghazali that is cut off. The first meaning is the literal sense of physical smallness, which is impossible for God; instead it is interpreted as 'the senses cannot perceive Him' – a negative attribute (tanzih). The second meaning is 'the one who knows the finest details of things', using the analogy of a craftsman with a 'subtle hand' – this makes al-Latif an attribute of knowledge. The third meaning is 'the One who is kind and gentle to His servants', providing for them in unexpected ways, supported by Qur'an 42:19. The fourth meaning from al-Ghazali begins but is incomplete in the text.

Key Terms
اللطيف
اللطف
البر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Allah's power
B) Allah's knowledge
C) Allah's mercy
D) Allah's justice

Key Takeaway
Al-Latif signifies both Allah's intricate knowledge and His gentle kindness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Four Divine Names of Knowledge: Permissible and Impermissible
Enumeration of four knowledge-related divine names with permissibility rulings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which four knowledge-related divine names are discussed by the author and why two of them are not permissible for Allah.

Core Idea
Not all Arabic intensives for knowledge can be applied to Allah.

This passage from Al-Matalib al-Aliyah presents an enumeration of four divine names related to knowledge. The author cites Quranic verses for each and clarifies that two of these words are not used for Allah due to implications of imperfection. Let's examine the reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the four names and the reasoning given for each.
2
Identify the permissible and impermissible names
From the passage, identify which names are permissible for Allah and which are not. Note the specific reasons: for al-`Ilm it suggests baseness; for al-`Allāmah it implies effort and acquisition.
3
Reflect on the theological principle
Ask yourself: Why must divine names be free from any implication of deficiency? The passage illustrates that even a word like 'allāmah, though seemingly intensive, is unsuitable because it implies change from deficiency to perfection.
This connects to the broader principle of tanzih (transcendence).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ورابعها : الأعلم . قال تعالى : ( ربكم أعلم بكم )(1).
وخامسها : العلم . قال تعالى : ( الرحمن علم القرآن ) (۲) و ( علمك ما لم تكن تعلم ) (۳) إلا أن هذا اللفظ لا يذكر في حق الله تعالى ، لأنه لفظ يوهم نوعاً من الدناءة .
وسادسها : العليم . قال تعالى : ( وأسروا قولكم أو اجهروا به . إنه عليم بذات الصدور ) (٤)
وسابعها : العلامة . يقال : رجل علامة إذا وصف بكثرة العلم ، ولا يجوز استعماله في حق الله تعالى . لأن هذه اللفظة وضعت لمن خرج عن القلة والنقصان إلى الكثرة والكمال ، بسبب التكلف والارتباض . وذلك في حق الله تعالى محال .
The fourth: al-A`lam (the Most Knowing). He said (the Most High): (Your Lord knows you best) (1). And the fifth: al-`Ilm (Knowledge). He said (the Most High): (The Most Merciful taught the Quran) (2) and (He taught you what you did not know) (3) except that this word is not mentioned regarding Allah the Most High, because it suggests a kind of baseness. And the sixth: al-`Alīm (the All-Knowing). He said (the Most High): (Conceal your words or proclaim them; indeed He is All-Knowing of that within the breasts) (4) And the seventh: al-`Allāmah (the very knowledgeable). It is said: a man is `allāmah when he is described as having abundant knowledge, but it is not permissible to use it regarding Allah the Most High. Because this word was coined for one who has gone from paucity and deficiency to abundance and perfection due to effort and practice. That is impossible regarding Allah the Most High.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is an enumeration and ruling regarding four divine names related to knowledge: al-A`lam, al-`Ilm, al-`Alīm, and al-`Allāmah. The author cites Quranic verses for each. For al-`Ilm (knowledge), he notes that the word itself is not used as a name for Allah because it implies a type of baseness (dana'a) when used in reference to a person. For al-`Allāmah, he explains that it is used for humans who acquire much knowledge through effort and practice, implying a transition from deficiency to perfection, which is impossible for Allah. The lesson guards against using derived forms that imply imperfection for Allah.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any Arabic intensive form like 'allāmah can be used as a divine name because it emphasizes knowledge.
Correction: The passage clarifies that 'allāmah is not permissible because it implies acquisition through effort and change from deficiency to perfection, which cannot be attributed to Allah.

Checkpoint

D) Because it implies ignorance.

Key Takeaway
Four knowledge-related divine names are discussed: al-A`lam and al-`Alīm are permissible, while al-`Ilm and al-`Allāmah are not due to implications of imperfection.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The name al-`Ilm is not used because it suggests baseness. What does that tell you about the importance of language when speaking of Allah? Will you be more careful in your own speech about Him starting today?

The Divine Name al-Latif: Meanings and Implications
Author enumerates four meanings of al-Latif and illustrates with a morsel example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four meanings of the divine name al-Latif and why it is uniquely attributed to Allah.

Core Idea
Al-Latif means the One whose knowledge and gentleness encompass all subtleties.

This passage explores the divine name al-Latif, beginning with the Quranic verse. The author explains four meanings, dismissing physical smallness as impossible for Allah, and instead emphasizing His transcendent knowledge, benevolence, and perfect gentleness. The lesson includes a powerful example of a simple morsel of food to illustrate Allah's subtle providence.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing, then read the literal translation. Notice the Quranic citation and the four numbered meanings.
Pay attention to the structure: each meaning is introduced with 'first', 'second', etc.
2
Examine the first meaning and its negation
The first meaning (physical smallness) is deemed impossible for Allah, so it is reinterpreted as transcendence: the senses cannot perceive Him. Understand how this becomes an attribute of tanzih.
This guards against anthropomorphism.
3
Understand the second and third meanings
The second meaning is deep knowledge of subtle matters; the third is extreme kindness to servants. Note how both are expressions of Allah's perfection.
These are positive attributes, in contrast to the first.
4
Study al-Ghazali's fourth meaning
Al-Ghazali combines perfect knowledge of benefits with gentle action. This meaning is only fully realized in Allah. Reflect on why this combination is unique to Him.
This integrates knowledge and action.
5
Reflect on the morsel example and conclusion
Read the example of the morsel of food. It shows how countless creatures cooperate, demonstrating Allah's subtlety. Note how the author ties the specific actions to divine names.
The example makes the abstract concept tangible.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثاني : اللطيف . قال تعالى : ( ألا يعلم من خلق ؟ وهو اللطيف الخبير ) (٥) واعلم أن « اللطيف ، له معاني . فالأول : أن الشيء الصغير جداً ، الذي لا يحس به لغاية صغره يسعى لطيفاً . وهذا في حق الله تعالى محال . فوجب حمله على لازمة ، وهو أن الحواس لا تقف عليه لكــونــه لطيفاً ، وبهذا التفسير يكون من صفات التنزيه . الثاني : اللطيف هو العالم بدقائق الأمور وغوامضها ، يقال فلان لطيف اليد. إذا كان حاذقاً في صنعته ، مهتدياً إلى ما يشكل على غيره ، وعلى هذا التقدير فكونه لطيفاً عبارة عن علمه . والثالث : اللطيف هو البر بعباده الذي يلطف بهم من حيث لا يعلمون ، وهييء مصالحهم من حيث إنهم لا يحتسبون ، ومنه قوله تعالى : الله لطيف بعباده ) () والرابع : قال الغزالي - رحمه الله - : وهذا الاسم إنما يستحقه من يعلم حقائق المصالح وغوامضها ، ثم يسلك في اتصالها إلى المستحقين سبيل الرفق دون العنف . فإذا اجتمع هذا العلم وهذا الفعل فقد تم معنى اللطيف ) .
ثم إنه لا يتصور كمال هذا العلم، وكمال هذا الفعل إلا لله - سبحانه - فإما علمه ـ سبحانه ـ بالدقائق فلا إشكال فيه لأن الخفي والجلي بالنسبة إلى علمه سيان . وأما رفقه في الأفعال ، ولطفه فيها ، فلا يدخل تحت الحصر . وههنا ( لا ) (١) يجب شرح أنواع حكم الله في خلق السموات ، والكواكب والعناصر الأربعة ، والمواليد الثلاثة ، بل يجب أن تكتفي ههنا بشرح لطفه في تيسير لقمة يتناولها العبد من غير كلفة ، فقد تعاون على إصلاحها خلق لا يحصى عددهم ، من مصلح الأرض وزارعها وساقيها وحاصد حبها ومنقيها وطاحتها وخابزها ، إلى غير ذلك ، فإن شرحها لا يتم في مجلدات ، فهو سبحانه من حيث دبر الأمور حكيم ، ومن حيث أوجدها : جواد ، ومن حيث رتبها : مصور ، ومن حيث وضع كل شيء في موضعه عدل ، ومن حيث لم يترك فيها دقائق وجوه الرفق لطيف ، ولن يعرف حقيقة لهذه الأسماء من لم يعرف حقيقة هذه الأفعال .
Second name: al-Laṭīf (the Subtle). He said (the Most High): (Does He who created not know? And He is the Subtle, the Aware) (5). Know that `al-Laṭīf' has meanings. The first: an extremely small thing that is not sensed due to its smallness is called laṭīf. This is impossible regarding Allah the Most High. So it must be taken in its necessary sense, which is that the senses do not perceive Him because He is Laṭīf (Subtle). With this interpretation, it is one of the attributes of transcendence. The second: al-Laṭīf is the one who knows the subtle points and intricacies of matters. It is said: so-and-so is laṭīf al-yad (skilled in hand) if he is dexterous in his craft, guided to what is difficult for others. On this estimation, His being Laṭīf is an expression of His knowledge. The third: al-Laṭīf is the Beneficent to His servants, who is kind to them in ways they do not know, and arranges their benefits in ways they do not anticipate. From it is His statement: (Allah is Laṭīf with His servants) (6). The fourth: al-Ghazālī (may Allah have mercy on him) said: This name is only deserved by one who knows the true benefits and their subtleties, then proceeds in delivering them to those who deserve them through gentleness rather than violence. When this knowledge and this action are combined, the meaning of al-Laṭīf is perfected. Then, the perfection of this knowledge and this action cannot be conceived except for Allah (glorified is He). As for His knowledge (glorified) of subtleties, there is no doubt because the hidden and the apparent are equal with respect to His knowledge. As for His gentleness in actions and subtlety in them, it is beyond enumeration. And here (it is not) necessary to explain the types of Allah's wisdom in creating the heavens, the planets, the four elements, and the three generated beings. Rather, one must suffice here with an explanation of His subtlety in facilitating a morsel that the servant consumes without effort. For creatures beyond count have cooperated in its preparation: from the cultivator of the earth, the sower, the irrigator, the reaper of its grain, the winnower, the miller, the baker, and so on. Their full explanation would not be completed in volumes. He (glorified) in terms of how He arranged matters is Ḥakīm (Wise); in terms of how He brought them into existence is Jawād (Generous); in terms of how He formed them is Muṣawwir (Fashioner); in terms of how He placed everything in its place is `Adl (Just); in terms of how He did not leave out the subtle aspects of gentleness is Laṭīf (Subtle). The reality of these names will not be known by one who does not know the reality of these actions.
Source pages p. 274

Commentary
This passage is a detailed exposition of the divine name al-Laṭīf. It begins by citing the Quranic verse from Surah al-Mulk. The author then explains that the term has multiple meanings. The first meaning (physical smallness) is impossible for Allah, so it must be interpreted as transcendence—that He is not perceived by the senses. The second meaning is deep knowledge, like a skilled artisan. The third meaning is extreme benevolence and kindness to servants in ways they do not perceive. The fourth meaning, from al-Ghazālī, combines perfect knowledge of benefits and delivering them gently. The author then argues that only Allah possesses this perfection of knowledge and action. He gives an extended example of a single morsel of food, showing how countless creatures cooperate in its production, illustrating Allah's subtle providence. He concludes each divine action necessitates a corresponding name, and true knowledge of these names depends on understanding the actions.

Key Terms

دقائق الأمور
صفات التنزيه
اللطف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some mistakenly think al-Laṭīf means physically small or that Allah is small.
Correction: The first meaning is rejected for Allah; instead, it signifies that He is not perceived by the senses (transcendence) and that He has perfect knowledge and gentle action.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The name al-Laṭīf encompasses Allah's transcendence, perfect knowledge of subtleties, and gentle, benevolent action — all uniquely combined in Him.

?

Two Interpretations of al-Khabir
Author gives two interpretations of al-Khabir.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two valid interpretations of the divine name al-Khabir and the theological nuance that distinguishes them from human knowledge.

Core Idea
Al-Khabir means either Allah knows all inner realities or He informs — never through testing.

In this lesson, we explore the divine name al-Khabir (the Aware). The author presents two interpretations that safeguard Allah's transcendence while affirming His perfect knowledge. Both interpretations are valid but require careful understanding to avoid attributing human limitations to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the author's wording.
Notice the two interpretations are introduced with 'الأول' and 'التفسير الثاني'.
2
Identify the first interpretation and its caution
The first interpretation links al-Khabir to deep knowledge of inner reality. Note the caution that human 'khibrah' is gained through testing, which does not apply to Allah.
The author explicitly says 'والله منزه عنه' (Allah is exalted above that).
3
Identify the second interpretation and its analogies
The second interpretation treats al-Khabir as 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ). Compare with the examples of al-Sami' and al-Badi' to understand the pattern.
This interpretation avoids any implication of acquired knowledge.
4
Reflect on why both interpretations are necessary
Consider how each interpretation protects Allah's transcendence while affirming His omniscience. Ask yourself how they complement each other.
Both interpretations are rooted in Arabic linguistic usage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث : الخبير . وله تفسيران: الأول : أن الخبير هو العالم يكنه الشيء المطلع على حقيقته ، يقال فلان بهذا الأمر خبير ، وله به خبرة ، إلا أن الخبرة في صفة المخلوقين إنما تستعمل في العلم الذي يتوصل إليه بالاختبار والامتحان . والله منزه عنه ، والتفسير الثاني : الخبير بمعنى المخبر كالسميع بمعنى المسمع ، والبديع بمعنى المبدع .
Third name: al-Khabīr (the Aware). It has two interpretations: The first: al-Khabīr is the one who knows the inner reality of a thing, acquainted with its true nature. It is said: so-and-so is khabīr in this matter, and he has khibrah (expertise) in it. However, khibrah in the attribute of created beings is only used for knowledge obtained through testing and examination. Allah is exalted above that. The second interpretation: al-Khabīr means 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ), like al-Samī` means 'the one who hears' (al-musmi`) and al-Badī` means 'the originator' (al-mubdi`).
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Khabīr with two interpretations. The first interprets al-Khabīr as the one who has deep knowledge of the inner reality (kinn) of things, similar to human expertise (khibrah). However, human expertise is attained through testing and examination, which does not apply to Allah. Thus this interpretation is valid but must be stripped of any implication of acquired knowledge. The second interpretation treats al-Khabīr as an active participle meaning 'the one who informs' (al-mukhbiṛ), analogous to al-Samī` (the All-Hearing) meaning 'the one who hears' or 'the one who causes to hear' (al-musmi`) and al-Badī` (the Incomparable) meaning 'the originator' (al-mubdi`).

Key Terms
الخبير
الخبرة
الاختبار
المخبر

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Khabir implies that Allah acquires knowledge through trial and experience, like humans.
Correction: The author clarifies that while human 'khibrah' is attained through testing, Allah is exalted above that. The first interpretation only affirms that Allah knows inner realities without any process of learning, and the second interpretation completely avoids this by meaning 'the one who informs'.

Checkpoint
What is the first interpretation of al-Khabīr?
A) The one who is patient.
B) The one who knows the inner reality of things.
C) The one who is knowledgeable from testing.
D) The one who informs.

Key Takeaway
The name al-Khabir is interpreted in two ways: as Allah's perfect inner knowledge (without testing) or as Allah's act of informing, both affirming His omniscience and transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The second interpretation says that Allah informs. Are you truly listening to what He informs you through the Qur’an and sunnah, or do you rely on your own experiences as if they teach Him? What message has He been informing you that you have ignored?
?
Think of a difficult situation you are currently facing. Have you unconsciously assumed that Allah is learning something from it? This lesson declares Him exalted above that. Will you repent from that assumption and instead ask Him to inform you of the wisdom in it?

Allah as al-Shahid: The Perfect Witness
Author introduces al-Shahid with Quranic verses and begins linguistic analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the divine name al-Shahid, its Quranic basis, and that it is an intensive form indicating Allah's complete and perfect witnessing over all things.

Core Idea
Al-Shahid reveals that Allah's witness is perfect and all-encompassing.

This lesson introduces the fourth divine name discussed by the author: al-Shahid (the Witness). We will read two Quranic verses that affirm this name and begin to explore its linguistic form, noting that it is an intensive form that emphasizes the perfection of Allah's witnessing. This foundation prepares us to appreciate the depth of this attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to hear the author's words. Then read the literal translation to grasp the meaning. Notice the two Quranic verses cited.
The passage ends mid-sentence due to a page break.
2
Identify the name and its verses
The author states that the fourth name is al-Shahid. He then quotes two verses: one from Surah Fussilat (41:53) and one from Surah Al-Imran (3:18). Reflect on how these verses highlight Allah's sufficiency and act of bearing witness.
3
Understand the intensive form
The author begins to explain that al-Shahid is an intensive form (mubālaghah) derived from shāhid (witness). This means it signifies an abundance or perfection of the attribute. Even though the explanation is cut short, this key linguistic point is clear.
Intensive forms in Arabic add emphasis and comprehensiveness.
4
Reflect on the sufficiency
The first verse asks: 'Is it not sufficient concerning your Lord that He is over all things a Witness?' Take a moment to consider what it means for Allah's witness to be sufficient for all matters.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع : الشهيد . قال تعالى : ( أولم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ) ؟(۳) وقال تعالى : شهد الله ) (۳) واعلم أن الشهيد مبالغة من
Fourth name: ash-Shahīd (the Witness). He said (the Most High): (Is it not sufficient concerning your Lord that He is, over all things, a Witness?) (3) And He said: (Allah witnesses) (3). And know that ash-Shahīd is an intensive form of...
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage begins the explanation of the divine name ash-Shahīd. The author cites two Quranic verses: one from Surah Fussilat asking about Allah's sufficiency as a witness, and another from Surah Al-Imran stating that Allah bears witness. The author then starts to explain that the word 'al-Shahīd' is an intensive form (mubālaghah) indicating abundance or perfection of witnessing, but the text is cut off here.

The Meaning of al-Latif: Knowledge and Gentle Action
The author defines al-Latif and illustrates it with the food provision example.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the divine name al-Latif encompasses both perfect knowledge of interests and gentle action, and why only Allah fully deserves this attribute.

Core Idea
Al-Latif means more than subtlety—it requires perfect knowledge and gentle kindness.

This passage from al-Matalib al-'Aliyah unpacks the rich meaning of the divine name al-Latif (the Gentle, the Subtle). The author sets a high bar for deserving this attribute: it requires both perfect insight into hidden benefits and the ability to deliver them with gentleness. Through a vivid example of how a simple morsel of food involves countless unseen acts of kindness, the author shows that only Allah can fully embody this name.

Guided Path
1
Grasp the definition of al-Latif
Read the first few lines to see that al-Latif is not just subtlety but a combination of knowledge and gentle action. Note the condition: knowing realities of interests and connecting them gently.
The term 'masalih' (interests) refers to benefits that bring good to creation.
2
Identify why only Allah qualifies
The passage argues that perfection in both knowledge and action is impossible for others because only Allah knows all subtleties and His gentle acts are beyond enumeration.
3
Meditate on the example of food
Consider the detailed chain of helpers preparing a single morsel—how it illustrates 'lutf' (gentle kindness) in provision without human effort.
The author uses this to show that even a simple act involves countless divine arrangements.
4
Connect the five names to actions
The author closes by linking wisdom, generosity, fashioning, justice, and gentleness to specific aspects of divine action, emphasizing that knowing names requires knowing these actions.
This shows that divine names are not abstract labels but reflect real actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إنما يستحقه من يعلم حقائق المصالح وغوامضها ، ثم يسلك في اتصالها إلى المستحقين سبيل الرفق دون العنف . فإذا اجتمع هذا العلم وهذا الفعل فقد تم معنى اللطيف ) . ثم إنه لا يتصور كمال هذا العلم، وكمال هذا الفعل إلا لله - سبحانه - فإما علمه ـ سبحانه ـ بالدقائق فلا إشكال فيه لأن الخفي والجلي بالنسبة إلى علمه سيان . وأما رفقه في الأفعال ، ولطفه فيها ، فلا يدخل تحت الحصر . وههنا ( لا ) (١) يجب شرح أنواع حكم الله في خلق السموات ، والكواكب والعناصر الأربعة ، والمواليد الثلاثة ، بل يجب أن تكتفي ههنا بشرح لطفه في تيسير لقمة يتناولها العبد من غير كلفة ، فقد تعاون على إصلاحها خلق لا يحصى عددهم ، من مصلح الأرض وزارعها وساقيها وحاصد حبها ومنقيها وطاحتها وخابزها ، إلى غير ذلك ، فإن شرحها لا يتم في مجلدات ، فهو سبحانه من حيث دبر الأمور حكيم ، ومن حيث أوجدها : جواد ، ومن حيث رتبها : مصور ، ومن حيث وضع كل شيء في موضعه عدل ، ومن حيث لم يترك فيها دقائق وجوه الرفق لطيف ، ولن يعرف حقيقة لهذه الأسماء من لم يعرف حقيقة هذه الأفعال .
He deserves [the attribute al-Latif] only the one who knows the realities of interests and their hidden depths, and then proceeds in connecting them to those deserving to the way of gentleness without harshness. So when this knowledge and this action are combined, the meaning of al-Latif is fulfilled. Then it is not conceivable that the perfection of this knowledge and the perfection of this action belong except to Allah - exalted is He. As for His knowledge - exalted is He - of the subtleties, there is no problem in it because the hidden and the manifest are equal in relation to His knowledge. And as for His gentleness in actions and His subtlety in them, it cannot be encompassed. And here it is not necessary to explain the types of Allah's wisdom in creating the heavens, the planets, the four elements, and the three generated beings, but rather one should be content here with an explanation of His gentleness in facilitating a morsel that the servant consumes without effort, for countless creatures have cooperated in its preparation: from the one who tills the earth, sows it, waters it, harvests its grain, cleans it, grinds it, and bakes it, and so on. For its explanation cannot be completed in volumes. So He - exalted is He - is wise in how He disposes affairs, generous in how He brings them into existence, fashioner in how He arranges them, just in how He places each thing in its place, and gentle in that He does not leave out the subtlest aspects of kindness. And one will not know the reality of these names unless one knows the reality of these actions.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage defines and exemplifies the divine name al-Latif (the Gentle/Subtle). The author begins by stating the condition for truly deserving this attribute: only one who perfectly knows the true interests and hidden benefits of things, and who then connects those benefits to their recipients with gentleness rather than harshness. When both perfect knowledge and gentle action are present, the full meaning of al-Latif is realized. Then the author argues that such perfection can only belong to Allah – His knowledge of subtleties is absolute (the hidden and apparent are equal to Him), and His gentle actions are beyond enumeration. Instead of listing all divine wisdom in creation, the author focuses on a simple example: the provision of a single morsel of food. Countless beings cooperate (tiller, sower, waterer, harvester, cleaner, grinder, baker) to prepare it without human effort. The author concludes by linking five divine names to corresponding actions: wisdom (tadbīr), generosity (ījād), fashioning (tartīb), justice (waḍ‘ kull shay’ fī mawḍi‘ihī), and gentleness (not leaving out any subtle kindness). One cannot truly know these names without understanding these actions.
