The Divine Name al-Khabir and Its Two Interpretations
The author defines al-Khabir with two interpretations: knower of inner secrets and active informer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two interpretations of the divine name al-Khabir and how they relate to Allah's exclusive knowledge.

Core Idea
Allah knows all hidden realities directly, not through testing.

In this lesson, we explore the divine name al-Khabir (the All-Aware). The author presents two complementary interpretations: one emphasizing Allah's perfect knowledge of inner secrets, the other highlighting His active role in informing creation. This dual perspective safeguards Allah's transcendence while affirming His communication with the world.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text once to hear the flow, then read the literal translation.
Notice the two interpretations are clearly separated by 'التفسير الثاني'.
2
Identify the two interpretations
List them: first, al-Khabir as knower of inner reality; second, as one who informs. Compare with human expertise.
The author contrasts human knowledge through testing with Allah's direct knowledge.
3
Understand the analogy with other names
The author links the second interpretation to al-Sami' and al-Badi', which have active meanings. This shows a pattern.
4
Apply the lesson
Reflect on how this name impacts your understanding of Allah's knowledge and communication.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الثالث : الخبير . وله تفسيران: الأول : أن الخبير هو العالم يكنه الشيء المطلع على حقيقته ، يقال فلان بهذا الأمر خبير ، وله به خبرة ، إلا أن الخبرة في صفة المخلوقين إنما تستعمل في العلم الذي يتوصل إليه بالاختبار والامتحان . والله منزه عنه ، والتفسير الثاني : الخبير بمعنى المخبر كالسميع بمعنى المسمع ، والبديع بمعنى المبدع .
The third name: al-Khabir. It has two interpretations: The first: that al-Khabir is the one who knows the inner nature of a thing, the one who is informed of its reality. It is said: So-and-so is khabir (expert) in this matter, and he has experience with it. However, experience when used as an attribute of creatures is only used for knowledge attained through testing and examination. Allah is exalted above that. The second interpretation: al-Khabir in the sense of 'one who informs' (mukhbi), just as al-Sami' means 'one who makes hear' (musmi') and al-Badi' means 'one who originates' (mubdi').
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Khabir (the All-Aware/Expert). The author gives two interpretations. The first: al-Khabir means the one who knows the inner reality and hidden core of a thing. Linguistically, 'khabīr' is used for a human expert who has knowledge through experience and testing. However, the author emphasizes that such a method is inapplicable to Allah – He knows directly without trial. The second interpretation treats al-Khabir as active participle meaning 'the one who informs' (mukhbi), analogous to al-Sami' (the Hearing) also meaning 'the one who makes hear' and al-Badi' (the Originator) meaning 'the one who originates'. So Allah informs creatures about hidden realities.

Key Terms

الخبرة
المخبر
السميع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah acquires knowledge through testing and learning like human experts.
Correction: Allah's knowledge is direct and innate; He does not need to test or examine to know. The term 'khabir' when used for humans implies experience, but for Allah it means perfect, immediate knowledge of hidden realities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
Al-Khabir means both the knower of all hidden realities (without testing) and the one who informs creation, as shown by analogies with other divine names.

The Four Interpretations of al-Shahid
The author enumerates four interpretations of al-Shahid with Quranic evidence.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the four interpretations of the divine name al-Shahid and how each relates to knowledge, presence, proof, and being testified to.

Core Idea
Al-Shahid means far more than just 'watching'—it encompasses knowledge, presence, evidence, and witness.

The name al-Shahid is one of Allah's beautiful names, but its meaning is deeper than simple observation. In this passage, the author unpacks four distinct interpretations, each drawing on Quranic usage. These range from knowledge of the apparent to the idea that Allah Himself is the One testified to by creation. Understanding these layers enriches our appreciation of divine witnessing.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the four interpretations
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author introduces each interpretation with a Quranic verse. Try to list the four interpretations in your own words.
The author uses 'the first', 'the second', etc., to structure the passage.
2
Compare each interpretation to its Quranic proof
For each interpretation, find the Quranic verse the author cites. Ask yourself how that verse supports the meaning being explained.
The first interpretation uses 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (6:73). The second uses 'whoever witnesses the month' (2:185). The third uses 'Allah witnesses that there is no deity except Him' (3:18). The fourth is not directly cited with a new verse but builds on the concept.
3
Notice the shift in the fourth interpretation
The fourth interpretation reverses the active sense: al-Shahid means 'the One testified to' rather than 'the One who testifies'. Reflect on how this passive meaning fits with the name.
This is similar to how some names like al-Mu'min can be understood actively or passively.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الرابع : الشهيد . قال تعالى : ( أولم يكف بربك أنه على كل شيء شهيد ) ؟(٢) وقال تعالى : شهد الله ) (٣) واعلم أن الشهيد مبالغة من الشاهد ، كالعليم من العالم، والقدير من القادر ، وفي تفسيره وجوه : الأول : إنه العالم . قال تعالى : ( عالم الغيب والشهادة ) (١) و ( الغيب : عبارة عما يظن و و الشهادة ، عما ظهر . فإذا اعتبر العلم مطلقاً فهو العليم ، وإذا أضيف إلى الغيب والأمور الباطنية فهو الخبير . وإذا أضيف إلى الأمور الظاهرة فهو الشهيد . الثاني : إن الشاهد والشهيد هو الحاضر المشاهد . قال تعالى : فمن شهد منكم الشهر فليصمه ) (٣) أي فمن حضر . وهذا الحضور إن كان بالعلم فهو الوجه الأول. وإن كان بالرؤية والإبصار كان وجهاً ثانياً . الثالث : الشهيد والشاهد ، هو الذي يثبت به الأمر المتنازع فيه بين الخصمين ، فكونه شهيداً معناه : أنه تعالى بين حقائق الأشياء ، وأظهرها ، ومن جملتها أنه بين توحيده وعدله وصفات جلاله بنصب الدلائل، ووضع البينات ، ومنه قوله : شهد الله أنه لا إله إلا هو ) ) والرابع : إنه تعالى شهيد بمعنى المشهود له وذلك لأن العباد يشهدون له بالوحدانية ، ويقرون له بالعبودية .
The fourth name: al-Shahid. He said, exalted: 'Is it not sufficient that your Lord is over all things a Witness?' (41:53) And He said, exalted: 'Allah witnesses' (3:18). And know that al-Shahid is an intensification of al-Shahid, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim and al-Qadir from al-Qadir. There are several interpretations: The first: that He is the All-Knowing. He said, exalted: 'Knower of the unseen and the witnessed' (6:73). Unseen refers to what is hidden, and the witnessed refers to what is apparent. So when knowledge is considered absolutely, it is al-'Alim; when added to the unseen and inner matters, it is al-Khabir; and when added to apparent matters, it is al-Shahid. The second: that al-Shahid is the present observer. He said: 'So whoever among you witnesses the month, let him fast it' (2:185), i.e., whoever is present. And this presence, if it is by knowledge, that is the first interpretation; and if it is by sight and vision, that is a second aspect. The third: that al-Shahid is the one through whom a disputed matter is established between two opponents. So His being a Witness means that He - exalted - makes the realities of things clear and manifests them, and among that is that He manifests His oneness, His justice, and the attributes of His majesty by setting up proofs and establishing evidences. And from this is His saying: 'Allah witnesses that there is no deity except Him' (3:18). The fourth: that He - exalted - is a Witness in the sense of 'the One testified to', because the servants testify to His oneness and acknowledge His servitude.
Source pages p. 275

Commentary
This passage lists and explains four interpretations of the divine name al-Shahid (the Witness). The author first notes that al-Shahid is an intensive form (mubālagha) of shāhid, similar to 'alīm from 'ālim and qadīr from qādir. Then he presents four aspects: (1) Al-Shahid means 'the All-Knowing' specifically regarding apparent things. The author distinguishes between al-'Alim (absolute knowledge), al-Khabir (knowledge of the hidden), and al-Shahid (knowledge of the apparent). (2) Al-Shahid means 'the present observer' – either by knowledge (which reverts to the first interpretation) or by physical sight. (3) Al-Shahid is the one through whom a dispute is settled – by making realities clear and manifesting proofs, such as the evidence for tawḥīd. (4) Al-Shahid means 'the One testified to' – because servants testify to His oneness and servitude. This final interpretation flips the active meaning to a passive one, similar to some views of other names.

الشاهد

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Shahid only means 'watching' or 'present'.
Correction: It also means knowledge of the apparent, establishing proof, and being testified to.

Checkpoint

A) For knowledge of hidden things
B) For knowledge of apparent things

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Shahid encompasses multiple meanings: knowledge of the apparent, presence, establishing proof, and being testified to by creation.

Allah is the One who clarifies disputed matters by setting up proofs. The Divine Name al-Hafiz: Two Meanings
Author explains al-Hafiz as an intensive form with two meanings.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two meanings of the divine name al-Hafiz: as the One who never forgets (knowledge) and the One who preserves from loss (action).

Core Idea
al-Hafiz combines perfect knowledge and active preservation.

The name al-Hafiz appears in multiple Qur'anic verses, each highlighting a different facet of preservation. This lesson unpacks two key meanings: first, that Allah never forgets anything, which relates to His perfect knowledge; second, that He actively preserves creation from loss or waste. Understanding both enriches our comprehension of this divine attribute.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two meanings
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice that the author explicitly states 'al-Hafiz has two meanings'. Underline or note each meaning as you read.
The first meaning is related to forgetfulness, the second to loss.
2
Understand the first meaning: knowledge and forgetfulness
The first meaning is 'contrary to forgetfulness and inadvertence'. The author explains this returns to the attribute of knowledge. Allah knows everything, completely and in detail, never subject to forgetting.
This connects al-Hafiz to the attribute of knowledge.
3
Understand the second meaning: active preservation from loss
The second meaning is 'whose opposite is loss/waste'. Allah preserves the heavens and the earths, maintaining their existence and order. This is an active, continuous preservation.
The verse from Surat al-Baqarah (2:255) is cited for this meaning.
4
Reflect on the combination of the two meanings
Consider how al-Hafiz encompasses both knowledge and action. Allah knows all things perfectly (never forgets) and preserves them perpetually (never loses or wastes). This dual aspect enriches our understanding of divine care.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم الخامس : الحفيظ . قال تعالى : ( ولا يؤوده حفظهما ) (٤) وقال تعالى : ( فالله خير حافظاً ) () وقال : ( إنا نحن نزلنا الذكر ، وإنـا لـه لحافظون ) (1) واعلم أن الحفيظ مبالغة من الحافظ كالعليم من العالم . وللحفيظ معنيان: أحدهما: ضد النسيان والسهو، ويرجع معناه إلى العلم فهو تعالى حفيظ للأشياء بمعنى كونه تعالى عالماً بجملها وتفاصيلها ، لا يتبدل بالسهو والنسيان . والثاني : الحفيظ . هو الذي ضده التضييع ، فهو سبحانه حافظ السموات والأرضين . قال تعالى : ( ولا يؤوده حفظهما )
The fifth name: al-Hafiz. He said, exalted: 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255) and He said: 'Allah is the best preserver' (12:64) and He said: 'Indeed We have sent down the Reminder, and indeed We will preserve it' (15:9). And know that al-Hafiz is an intensification of al-Hafiz, like al-'Alim from al-'Alim. Al-Hafiz has two meanings: One: contrary to forgetfulness and inadvertence, and its meaning returns to knowledge. So He - exalted - is Hafiz of things in the sense of being Knower of their wholes and their details, not subject to forgetfulness and inadvertence. The second: al-Hafiz is the one whose opposite is loss/waste. So He - exalted - is the Preserver of the heavens and the earths. He said: 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255).
Source pages p. 276

Commentary
This passage explains the divine name al-Hafiz (the Preserver). The author first notes that al-Hafiz is an intensive form, like 'alīm. Then he presents two meanings. The first meaning relates to knowledge: al-Hafiz means 'the One who never forgets'. It is opposite to forgetfulness and inadvertence. This meaning ultimately returns to the attribute of knowledge: Allah knows all things in their entirety and detail, never subject to forgetfulness. The second meaning relates to action: al-Hafiz means 'the One who preserves from loss'. Its opposite is 'tadī‘' (waste/loss). Allah preserves the heavens and the earths from destruction, as stated in the verse. The author cites the verse 'He is not wearied by their preservation' (2:255) which applies to both meanings.

Key Terms

الحافظ
النسيان
السهو
التضييع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think al-Hafiz only means 'protector' from harm, without the aspect of knowledge.
Correction: The author shows that the first meaning of al-Hafiz is 'contrary to forgetfulness', which directly relates to Allah's perfect knowledge, not just active protection.

Checkpoint

A) Preserving from physical harm
B) Preserving from forgetfulness (i.e., knowing all details perfectly)
C) Preserving from punishment
D) Preserving the Quran

Key Takeaway
The divine name al-Hafiz encompasses both perfect knowledge (never forgetting) and active preservation (never losing or wasting).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
You often worry about losing your health, wealth, or loved ones. But Allah is the One who preserves the heavens and earths without weariness. Is your anxiety rooted in a lack of trust that He can preserve what matters most to you? What would change today if you truly believed that His preservation never fails?
?

The Divine Name al-Raqīb and the Key of Watchfulness
Sanusi explains the divine name al-Raqīb and the servant's duty of watchfulness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name al-Raqīb and how the servant's constant awareness of Allah's watchfulness is the key to all goodness.

Core Idea
Allah's watchfulness inspires the servant's awareness, unlocking every good deed.

Sanusi now explains the divine name al-Raqīb (the Watchful). He anchors it in the Qur'an and then draws a profound distinction: the servant's murāqaba is not God's attribute but the servant's own knowledge that God is always aware. This awareness, he teaches, unlocks every good deed. Let us see how he builds this concept.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how Sanusi first defines al-Raqīb using Quranic verses and al-Khaṭṭābī's explanation, then shifts to the servant's murāqaba.
The passage has two clear sections: definition of the divine name and application for the servant.
2
Distinguish divine attribute from servant action
Identify that al-Raqīb is God's attribute (the Preserver, the Watchful), while murāqaba is the servant's action—his knowledge that God is aware of him. This distinction is crucial.
The word murāqaba comes from the same root but refers to the servant's state.
3
Reflect on why murāqaba is the key to all good
Sanusi says that when the servant is certain of God's watchfulness over his states, thoughts, and words, he fears God and stands in awe. This state leads to all good deeds.
Consider how this awareness transforms one's actions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الرقيب ، قال تعالى حكاية عن عيسى عليه السلام : ( فلما توفيتني كنت أنت الرقيب عليهم ) والرقيب الحافظ الذي لا يغيب عنه شيء. ومنه قوله تعالى : ( ما يلفظ من قول إلا لديه رقيب عتيد ) (4) قال الخطابي : وهو في نعوت الآدميين : الموكل بحفظ الشيء المترصد له ، المحترز عن الغفلة فيه . وأما مراقبة العبد ربه فهي علم العبد باطلاع الحق عليه ، فاستدامته لهذا العلم هي المسماة بمراقبة الله تعالى . وهذه المراقبة مفتاح كل الخيرات ، فإن العبد إذا تيقن أن الله مراقب لأحواله ، مطلع على ضمائره ، سامع لأقواله ، لا يغيب عن علمه النقير والقطمير والصغير والكبير ، خاف سطوات عقابه في كل حال، وهابه في كل مقال ، عالما بأنه الحكيم الرقيب ، والشاهد الذي لا يغيب
The sixth name: al-Raqīb (the Watchful). He said, exalted, in the story of Jesus, peace be upon him: (When You took me up, You were the Watchful over them) (5:117). And al-Raqīb is the Preserver from whom nothing is hidden. And from it is His saying, exalted: (Not a word does he utter but that there is beside him a Raqīb (watcher) ready) (50:18). Al-Khaṭṭābī said: And it (al-Raqīb) among the descriptions of humans is the one entrusted with preserving something, observing it, cautious about heedlessness regarding it. As for the servant’s murāqaba (watchfulness) of his Lord, it is the servant’s knowledge of the Truth’s awareness of him, so his continuous maintenance of this knowledge is called murāqabah of God, exalted. And this murāqabah is the key to all good things, for if the servant is certain that God is Watchful over his states, Aware of his innermost thoughts, Hearing his words, that not a small thing nor a large thing is absent from His knowledge, he fears the onslaught of His punishment in every condition, and stands in awe of Him in every speech, knowing that He is the Wise Watchful, and the Witness from whom nothing is hidden.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Raqīb (the Watchful). The author begins with a Quranic verse from the story of Jesus where Jesus says to God, 'You were the Raqīb over them.' He then defines al-Raqīb as the Preserver from whom nothing is hidden, citing another verse about a watcher (raqīb) beside every person. He quotes al-Khaṭṭābī's explanation that for humans, raqīb means one entrusted with preserving something, observing it carefully. Then the author distinguishes the servant’s murāqaba (watchfulness) of God, which is the servant’s knowledge that God is aware of him. Maintaining this knowledge is called murāqabah, and it is the key to all good. When a servant is certain that God watches his states, knows his innermost thoughts, hears his words, and nothing is absent from His knowledge, he fears God’s punishment in every situation and stands in awe of Him, knowing that God is al-Ḥakīm al-Raqīb (the Wise Watchful) and al-Shāhid (the Witness) who never forgets.

The Divine Name al-Muhaymin and Its Three Combined Attributes
Author explains the divine name al-Muhaymin as combining perfect knowledge, power, and continuous care.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three combined attributes that constitute the divine name al-Muhaymin and why only Allah can possess them perfectly.

Core Idea
Al-Muhaymin uniquely combines perfect knowledge, power, and constant care.

Sanusi now turns to the seventh divine name, al-Muhaymin. He draws from the Quranic verse where Allah is described as 'al-Salām, al-Mu'min, al-Muhaymin'. In this passage, he unpacks the meaning of al-Muhaymin by showing it is not a simple attribute but a composite of three distinct qualities. Understanding this name deepens our appreciation of Allah's comprehensive guardianship.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis. Then read the literal translation. Notice the Quranic citation at the beginning.
The verse is from Surah al-Hashr (59:23).
2
Identify the three attributes
The passage lists three attributes that combine to form the meaning of al-Muhaymin. Find each one and note what it refers to.
The attributes are: knowledge of the state, complete power, and constant perseverance.
3
Reflect on the exclusivity to Allah
The author concludes that these attributes can only be perfectly combined in Allah. Think about why this is logically necessary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السابع : المهيمن . قال تعالى : ( السلام المؤمن المهيمن ) (1) وهو اسم لمن كان موصوفاً بمجموع صفات ثلاثة : أحدها : العلم بأحوال الشيء . والثاني : القدرة التامة على تحصيل مصالح ذلك الشيء . والثالث : المواظبة على تحصيل تلك المصالح، فالجامع لهذه المعاني : اسم المهيمن . ولن تجتمع هذه الصفات على الكمال إلا الله - سبحانه .
The seventh name: al-Muhaymin (the Guardian, the Preserver). He said, exalted: (The Peace, the Faithful, the Guardian) (59:23). And it is a name for the one who is described by a combination of three attributes: one of them: knowledge of the state of a thing. The second: complete power to obtain the interests (maṣāliḥ) of that thing. And the third: constant perseverance in obtaining those interests. So the one who combines these meanings is the name al-Muhaymin. And these attributes cannot come together perfectly except for God, glorified.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines the divine name al-Muhaymin (the Guardian). The author cites the Quranic verse from Surah al-Hashr where God is described as 'al-Salām, al-Mu'min, al-Muhaymin'. He then explains that al-Muhaymin is a name for one who is described by three combined attributes: First, knowledge of the state of a thing (knowing what is good or harmful). Second, complete power to achieve the interests (maṣāliḥ) of that thing (ability to bring about benefit). Third, constant perseverance in achieving those interests (continuous care). The name al-Muhaymin is the one that encompasses these three meanings. The author concludes that these attributes can only be found perfectly together in God, the Exalted. Thus al-Muhaymin uniquely denotes God’s perfect knowledge, power, and perpetual care.

Correction: The passage shows that al-Muhaymin specifically means one who has perfect knowledge, complete power, and constant care, and these are exclusive to Allah.

The Divine Name ar-Raqeeb and the Key of Muraqabah
Explains the divine name ar-Raqeeb with Quranic evidence and commentary.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the meaning of the divine name ar-Raqeeb, its Quranic evidence, and how the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) is the key to all goodness.

Core Idea
ar-Raqeeb is the ever-watchful Guardian; muraqabah is the key to all good.

This lesson explores the divine name ar-Raqeeb, which combines vigilant observation with active guardianship. We will see how the Qur'an presents this attribute and how the early scholar al-Khattabi distinguished its human and divine senses. Most importantly, we learn that the servant's constant awareness of Allah's watchfulness—called muraqabah—is the foundation of all piety and goodness.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition and Quranic proof
Start by reading the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice how the author first defines ar-Raqeeb as 'the Guardian from whom nothing is absent' and then supports it with two Quranic verses.
The first verse is about Prophet Jesus (peace be upon him).
2
Understand al-Khattabi's distinction
Al-Khattabi explains that when the term 'raqeeb' is used for humans, it means one entrusted with guarding. Contrast this with the divine sense, which implies perfect and constant awareness without negligence.
This helps avoid anthropomorphism.
3
Grasp the concept of muraqabah
The passage defines the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) as the knowledge that Allah is fully aware of him, and continuing in that awareness. This is called muraqabah of Allah.
Muraqabah is a state of heart, not just an action.
4
Reflect on the key to all good
The passage declares that this muraqabah is the key to all good. Because when a servant is certain that Allah watches everything—inner thoughts, words, small and big—he fears Allah and is awed, leading to righteousness.
List the areas of Allah's watchfulness mentioned: states, innermost thoughts, words.
5
Test your understanding
Use the checkpoint question to confirm you can identify the main point from the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الإسم السادس : الرقيب ، قال تعالى حكاية عن عيسى عليه السلام : ( فلما توفيتني كنت أنت الرقيب عليهم ) والرقيب الحافظ الذي لا يغيب عنه شيء. ومنه قوله تعالى : ( ما يلفظ من قول إلا لديه رقيب عتيد ) قال الخطابي : وهو في نعوت الآدميين : الموكل بحفظ الشيء المترصد له ، المحترز عن الغفلة فيه . وأما مراقبة العبد ربه فهي علم العبد باطلاع الحق عليه ، فاستدامته لهذا العلم هي المسماة بمراقبة الله تعالى . وهذه المراقبة مفتاح كل الخيرات ، فإن العبد إذا تيقن أن الله مراقب لأحواله ، مطلع على ضمائره ، سامع لأقواله ، لا يغيب عن علمه النقير والقطمير والصغير والكبير ، خاف سطوات عقابه في كل حال، وهابه في كل مقال ، عالما بأنه الحكيم الرقيب ، والشاهد الذي لا يغيب
The sixth name: ar-Raqeeb (the Watcher). He, exalted, said, narrating from Jesus, peace be upon him: 'When You took me up, You were the Watcher over them.' And ar-Raqeeb is the Guardian from whom nothing is absent. And from it is His saying, exalted: 'Not a word does he utter but there is a Watcher by him ready.' Al-Khattabi said: It, in the attributes of humans, is the one entrusted with guarding something, watching for it, guarding against negligence concerning it. As for the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) of his Lord, it is the servant's knowledge of the Truth's awareness of him, and his continuance in this knowledge is what is called watchfulness (muraqabah) of Allah, exalted. And this watchfulness is the key to all good things. For when the servant is certain that Allah is watching over his states, aware of his innermost thoughts, hearing his words, and that nothing, be it a speck or a tiny thing, small or great, is absent from His knowledge, he fears the blows of His punishment in every state, and holds Him in awe in every speech, knowing that He is the Wise, the Watcher, the Witness who does not disappear.
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage defines and explains the divine name ar-Raqeeb (the Watcher). It begins by giving the definition: ar-Raqeeb is the Guardian from whom nothing is absent, supported by Quranic verses. Then it quotes Al-Khattabi who explains the human attribute of being a watcher (raqeeb) as one entrusted with guarding and watching for negligence. The passage then distinguishes this from the servant's watchfulness (muraqabah) of Allah, which is the servant's awareness that Allah is fully aware of him. This awareness, when constantly maintained, is called muraqabah and is described as the key to all good. The reason is that certainty of Allah's comprehensive watchfulness—over states, innermost thoughts, and words, and that nothing is hidden from Him—instills fear and awe in the servant, leading to piety.

Correction: The passage clarifies that ar-Raqeeb is the active Guardian who is never negligent and from whom nothing is absent, implying both watchfulness and guardianship.

The Name al-Muhaymin and Its Three Attributes
Defines the divine name al-Muhaymin as a combination of three attributes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the divine name al-Muhaymin combines three specific attributes: knowledge, complete power, and continuous care.

Core Idea
Al-Muhaymin means perfect knowledge, power, and relentless care.

Divine names often encapsulate deep meanings. In this lesson, we explore the name al-Muhaymin (the Dominant) as defined by Imam al-Razi. The passage breaks down this name into three essential attributes, showing that it is not a simple title but a composite of perfect qualities. Understanding this helps us appreciate the comprehensiveness of Allah's care.

The Infinite Effects of Allah's Power and the Multiplicity of His Names
Explains the first type: infinite effects of divine power lead to many names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah has many names corresponding to the infinite varieties of His creation.

Core Idea
Allah's names reflect the boundless manifestations of His power and wisdom.

This passage, a concluding discussion of the chapter, reveals the profound connection between the diversity of creation and the multitude of divine names. The author explains that the almost infinite effects of Allah's power necessitate a name for each type, and deeper knowledge of creation enhances knowledge of Allah's names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Identify the cause and effect

This is the 'secret' and 'mainstay' of the chapter.

واعلم أنا لما انتهينا إلى هذا الموضع عرض ما أوجب قطع هذا النوع من الكلام . ونختم هذا الباب بنوعين من البحث : أما النوع الأول : فهو أن تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في خلق الأجناس والأنواع والأصناف والأشخاص بحسب الذوات وبحسب الصفات ، ويحسب آثار الحكمة في تخليق تلك الذوات الموصوفة بتلك الصفات المخصوصة تكاد تكون غير متناهية ، ولهذا السبب جعل الله تعالى بحسب كل نوع منها اسم خاص . وكل من كان أكمل عرفاناً بأقسام مخلوقات الله تعالى ، وأكثر وقوفاً على آثار حكمته في تخليقها كان علمه بأسماء الله أكبر . فهذا هو السر ، والعمدة في هذا الباب . والنوع الثاني من البحث : أن هذه الرقى العزائم المذكورة بالألفاظ المجهولة ، إما أن تكون من أسماء الله أو من أسماء الملائكة أو من أسماء الشياطين . ولا شك أن أشرفها وأقواها في التأثير هو أسماء الله . ولما بينا أنها محصورة في هذه الأقسام التي لخصناها ، علمنا أن منتهى الأمر في تلك الألفاظ المجهولة أن تكون دالة على هذه الصفات ، فلعل السبب في تأثيرات تلك الألفاظ : أن قارئها إذا لم يكن عالماً بها فإنه يستولي على قلبه نوع خوف وفزع ، فيحصل في ذلك الوقت اتصال بجوهر النفس الناطقة بعالم المفارقات والمقدسات ، فتقوى التأثيرات . لهذا السبب ( والله أعلم بالصواب )
And know that when we reached this point, something appeared that necessitated cutting this type of discourse. And we conclude this chapter with two types of research: As for the first type: it is that the effect of the power of Allah, exalted, in the creation of genera, species, kinds, and individuals according to essences and according to attributes, and according to the traces of wisdom in the creation of those essences described with those particular attributes, is almost infinite. For this reason Allah, exalted, appointed for each type a specific name. And whoever is more perfect in knowledge of the divisions of Allah's creation, and more acquainted with the traces of His wisdom in their creation, has greater knowledge of Allah's names. This is the secret and the mainstay in this chapter. And the second type of research: that these incantations (ruqa) and spells mentioned with unknown words, are either from the names of Allah, or from the names of angels, or from the names of devils. There is no doubt that the most noble and most powerful in effect are the names of Allah. And since we have shown that they are limited to these divisions which we have summarized, we know that the ultimate matter in those unknown words is that they indicate these attributes. Perhaps the reason for the effects of those words is that when the reciter is not knowledgeable about them, a kind of fear and terror overcomes his heart, and at that time a connection occurs between the essence of the rational soul and the world of separate substances and holy things, so the effects become stronger. For this reason (and Allah knows best what is correct).
Source pages p. 277

Commentary
This passage provides two concluding discussions for the chapter. The first discussion explains why Allah has many names: because His power creates an almost infinite variety of beings, each with specific attributes and wisdom, and He appoints a name for each type. Thus, deeper knowledge of creation leads to greater knowledge of Allah's names. The second discussion addresses the use of unknown words in incantations (ruqa). These words must be names of Allah, angels, or devils. The most powerful are Allah's names. The author suggests that even if the words are unknown, they still denote divine attributes, and their effectiveness may stem from the fear and awe they induce in the reciter, causing a spiritual connection to the transcendent realm, thereby strengthening the intended effect. The passage ends with a disclaimer that Allah knows best.

Key Terms
قدرة الله
الأسماء
الحكمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Allah has only 99 names mentioned in hadith.
Correction: The passage indicates that Allah has names corresponding to every type of creation, which are numerous; the 99 are the most well-known.

Checkpoint
According to the first type of research, what is the reason for Allah having many names?
A) Because He likes variety
B) Because His power creates almost infinite types of beings
C) Because angels have many names
D) Because humans need many names

Key Takeaway
Allah's names are not arbitrary; they arise from the boundless manifestations of His power and wisdom in creation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You know that each type of creation reveals a name of Allah. When you look at the world today, how many of His names can you actually see? Not just memorize, but truly witness. What does the number—or the lack of it—say about your observation of His creation?
?
The more you study creation, the greater your knowledge of Allah's names. What specific aspect of creation have you ignored or neglected to study, and what does that reveal about your desire to know Him? Will you commit to learning one new thing about creation this week to increase your knowledge of His names?
?
If Allah's power is almost infinite in its effects, then your current understanding of His power is painfully limited. Does that thought humble you or frighten you? And what will you do today to step out of your comfort zone and reflect on a part of creation you have never considered before?

The Effectiveness of Unknown Words in Incantations
Explains the second type: the nature and effectiveness of unknown words in ruqa.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rationale behind the effectiveness of unknown words in ruqa and the author's explanation.

Core Idea
Unknown words in ruqa work through the divine attributes they signify and the spiritual state they induce.

In this second research type, the author addresses a practical question: how can unknown words in incantations be effective? He argues that such words must belong to one of three categories, and their power ultimately derives from Allah's names. Moreover, the reciter's lack of knowledge can produce a fear that facilitates a spiritual connection, enhancing the effect.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage for the second type
Read the Arabic passage carefully, noting the classification and the proposed mechanism.
Pay attention to the phrase 'فلعل السبب' which introduces the explanation.
2
Identify the three categories
The passage lists three possible origins for unknown words in ruqa: names of Allah, names of angels, or names of devils.
3
Understand the mechanism
The author suggests that the reciter's lack of knowledge causes fear and terror, which leads to a spiritual connection with the transcendent realm, making the effects stronger.
This is a psychological and spiritual explanation.
4
Reflect on the ultimate source
Even though the words are unknown, they ultimately indicate divine attributes, which is why they have power.

The First Type: Divine Names and Creation
The author explains the first type: linking divine names to creation's diversity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first type of research: how God's names are tied to the infinite diversity of creation.

Core Idea
God's names correspond to the vast variety of His creation.

The author begins by unveiling a profound connection: the more we comprehend the layers of creation, the deeper our knowledge of God's names. This lesson explores the first type of research into divine names.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author describes the scope of God's power in creation.
Pay attention to the list of categories: genera, species, kinds, individuals.
2
Identify the key insight
Find the sentence that states the direct relationship between knowledge of creation and knowledge of God's names.
This is the core of the first type.
3
Reflect on the implications
Consider why this connection is called 'the secret and the basis' of this topic.
Think about how this principle can be applied to deepen one's understanding of divine names.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما النوع الأول : فهو أن تأثير قدرة الله تعالى في خلق الأجناس والأنواع والأصناف والأشخاص بحسب الذوات وبحسب الصفات ، ويحسب آثار الحكمة في تخليق تلك الذوات الموصوفة بتلك الصفات المخصوصة تكاد تكون غير متناهية ، ولهذا السبب جعل الله تعالى بحسب كل نوع منها اسم خاص . وكل من كان أكمل عرفاناً بأقسام مخلوقات الله تعالى ، وأكثر وقوفاً على آثار حكمته في تخليقها كان علمه بأسماء الله أكبر . فهذا هو السر ، والعمدة في هذا الباب .
والنوع الثاني من البحث : أن هذه الرقى العزائم المذكورة بالألفاظ المجهولة ، إما أن تكون من أسماء الله أو من أسماء الملائكة أو من أسماء الشياطين . ولا شك أن أشرفها وأقواها في التأثير هو أسماء الله . ولما بينا أنها محصورة في هذه الأقسام التي لخصناها ، علمنا أن منتهى الأمر في تلك الألفاظ المجهولة أن تكون دالة على هذه الصفات ، فلعل السبب في تأثيرات تلك الألفاظ : أن قارئها إذا لم يكن عالماً بها فإنه يستولي على قلبه نوع خوف وفزع ، فيحصل في ذلك الوقت اتصال بجوهر النفس الناطقة بعالم المفارقات والمقدسات ، فتقوى التأثيرات . لهذا السبب ( والله أعلم بالصواب )
As for the first type: it is that the effect of the power of God, the Exalted, in creating the genera, species, kinds, and individuals, according to essences and according to attributes, and according to the effects of wisdom in forming those essences described with those specific attributes, is almost infinite. For this reason, God, the Exalted, assigned for each type among them a specific name. And whoever has more complete knowledge of the divisions of God's creatures, and more awareness of the effects of His wisdom in their formation, his knowledge of God's names is greater. This is the secret and the basis in this chapter.

The second type of research: that these ruqyah and incantations mentioned with unknown words are either from the names of God, or from the names of angels, or from the names of devils. There is no doubt that the most noble and strongest in effect are the names of God. Since we have shown that they are restricted to these categories which we have summarized, we know that the final outcome of those unknown words is that they indicate these attributes. Perhaps the reason for the effects of those words is that if the reader is not knowledgeable about them, a kind of fear and terror overwhelms his heart, and at that time a connection occurs between the essence of the rational soul and the world of the separated and sanctified beings, so the effects become strong. For this reason (and God knows best what is correct).
Source pages p. 278

Commentary
This passage presents two types of investigation concerning the names of God and the unknown words used in ruqyah (incantations). The first type focuses on the relationship between God's power and the diversity of creation. The author explains that God's power in creating various genera, species, kinds, and individuals—considering their essences, attributes, and the effects of wisdom in their formation—is virtually limitless. Consequently, God assigned a specific name for each type of creation. The more one understands the divisions of God's creatures and the signs of His wisdom in their creation, the greater one's knowledge of God's names. This, the author states, is the secret and foundation of this topic.

The second type addresses the unknown words found in ruqyah and incantations. These words are categorized as either names of God, names of angels, or names of devils. The most noble and effective are the names of God. Since these words are confined to these categories, they ultimately refer to divine attributes. The author suggests that the effect of these words arises because if the reciter does not understand them, fear and awe overtake the heart, leading to a connection between the rational soul and the transcendent, sacred realm, thereby strengthening the effects. The author concludes with 'and God knows best what is correct.' This passage thus combines theophany, epistemology, and the psychology of spiritual practice.

Key Terms
الرقى العزائم
الألفاظ المجهولة
جوهر النفس الناطقة
عالم المفارقات والمقدسات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first type of research, what is the relationship between knowledge of creation and knowledge of God's names?
A) They are unrelated.
B) Knowledge of creation decreases knowledge of names.
C) More knowledge of creation leads to greater knowledge of names.
D) Only prophets know both.

Key Takeaway
The first type of research reveals that God's names are intrinsically linked to the diversity of creation, and knowledge of creation enhances knowledge of the divine names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you look at the vast variety of creation—from the smallest insect to the complexity of the human soul—do you see them as random accidents or as deliberate signs pointing to specific names of God? Which single name of God has recently become more real to you because of something you observed in creation? Will you take a moment today to seek that connection?
?
The author says that knowledge of God's names grows with awareness of His wisdom in creation. Is your study of creation driven by curiosity about God, or by mere fascination with the world? What would change if you approached every natural phenomenon as a lesson about a divine name?
?
If it is true that each type of creation corresponds to a specific name of God, then neglecting to reflect on creation means neglecting the names of God. Do you spend more time memorizing the names or contemplating the creation that reveals them? What one step can you take this week to bridge that gap?

The Second Type: Unknown Words in Ruqyah
The author classifies unknown ruqyah words and explains their effect through fear and transcendent connection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the second type of research: the classification and mechanism of effect of unknown words in ruqyah.

Core Idea
Unknown ruqyah words derive power from fear and a transcendent connection.

The second type of research delves into the mysterious words used in ruqyah. The author categorizes their origin and proposes a spiritual-psychological explanation for their effectiveness.

Defining al-Hikmah and Distinguishing Divine Motives
Al-Razi defines al-hikmah in two senses and discusses the divine motive.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the two senses of al-hikmah (theoretical and practical) and the distinction between the motive of appetite and the motive of wisdom, and why the latter applies to God.

Core Idea
God's wisdom is altruistic, not appetitive.

Al-Razi begins this chapter by clarifying what wisdom (al-hikmah) means, distinguishing two types: theoretical wisdom as knowledge of realities, and practical wisdom as action leading to benefit. He then relates these to the motives behind actions—appetite (self-benefit) and wisdom (benefit to others)—and explains that since God is free from appetite, only the motive of wisdom can apply. This sets the stage for the proofs of God's wisdom from the philosophers and Mu'tazilites.

Guided Path
1
Understand the two definitions of al-hikmah
Read the passage where wisdom is defined theoretically and practically. Note that theoretical wisdom is knowledge, and practical wisdom is action that brings benefit and repels harm.
God's knowledge is the most perfect, so His theoretical wisdom is supreme.
2
Distinguish the two motives
Identify the motive of appetite (self-benefit) and the motive of wisdom (benefit to others). Understand that these are distinguished by the intended recipient of the benefit or harm.
The motive of wisdom is altruistic, not selfish.
3
Apply to God
Realize that God is transcendent and free from appetite, aversion, pain, and pleasure. Therefore, the motive of appetite cannot apply to Him. The remaining task is to prove that the motive of wisdom applies to God.
The author then introduces two ways to prove this: the philosophers' way and the Mu'tazilites' way.

Chapter Six: Proving that He, exalted, is Wise. Know that wisdom (al-hikmah) is explained in two ways: one of them is theoretical wisdom (al-hikmah an-nazariyyah), which is knowledge of the realities of things. And the knowledge of God, exalted, is the most perfect and most excellent of sciences, so it is necessary that His wisdom be the best and most complete of the types of wisdom. The second is practical wisdom (al-hikmah al-'amaliyyah), which is an expression of the action that leads to obtaining benefit and repelling harm. Then we say: if the intent of that action is to bring benefit to oneself and repel harm from oneself, then this is what is called the motive of appetite (da'iyat ash-shahwah). And if the intent of that action is to bring benefit to another and repel harm from another, then this is what is called the motive of wisdom (da'iyat al-hikmah). And since it has been established by proofs that God, exalted, is free from appetite and aversion, and from pain and pleasure, the motive of appetite is impossible to be established for Him. The discussion remains in proving the motive of wisdom for God, exalted. So we say: for the philosophers there is a way to establish this claim, and for the Mu'tazilites there is another way. And we will mention each one in the best manner. As for the philosophers' way: it is that they said: the intellect judges that existence ... but
Source pages p. 279

Commentary
This passage defines the concept of wisdom (al-hikmah) in two senses and sets up the discussion of how to prove that God acts with wisdom while being free from motives of appetite. The author first explains that wisdom is interpreted in two ways: theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of the realities of things, and practical wisdom, which is action that brings benefit and repels harm. Since God's knowledge is the most perfect, His wisdom is the most excellent. Then he distinguishes between two kinds of motives behind actions: the motive of appetite, where the agent seeks benefit or repels harm for himself, and the motive of wisdom, where the agent seeks benefit or repels harm for others. Because God is transcendent and free from appetite, aversion, pain, and pleasure, the motive of appetite cannot apply to Him. The remaining task is to prove that the motive of wisdom applies to God. The author announces that there are two ways to do this: the way of the philosophers and the way of the Mu'tazilites, and he will present each. The passage ends as he begins to outline the philosophers' argument that the intellect judges about existence...

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that God's wisdom implies He has human-like motives or desires.
Correction: The passage clarifies that God's wisdom is not motivated by appetite (self-interest) but by altruistic wisdom, befitting His transcendence.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the motive called when an action aims to bring benefit to oneself and repel harm from oneself?
A) The motive of wisdom (da'iyat al-hikmah)
B) The motive of appetite (da'iyat ash-shahwah)
C) The motive of knowledge
D) The motive of pleasure

Key Takeaway
God's wisdom is proven by the motive of wisdom, which is altruistic and befits His transcendence.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform an act of kindness, do you honestly check whether your motive is truly for the other person's benefit (dā'iyat al-ḥikmah) or secretly for your own reward or reputation (dā'iyat al-shahwah)? What would it take for you to act with pure wisdom today?
?
If God's wisdom is utterly selfless and free from any need, how does that challenge your image of worship? Do you sometimes treat God as if He needs your prayers, rather than seeing worship as a wise act that benefits you and others?
?
Think of a situation where you claimed to help someone 'for their sake' but later realized you had a hidden self-interest. What specific step can you take right now to align your intentions with the motive of wisdom as defined in this lesson?

Defining Wisdom and Its Types
Al-Razi defines wisdom and distinguishes motives.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the twofold definition of wisdom and how it applies to God, distinguishing between the motive of desire and the motive of wisdom.

Core Idea
Wisdom is both knowing realities and acting for the benefit of others.

Before we can prove that God is wise, we must first agree on what 'wisdom' means. Fakhr al-Din al-Razi presents two senses: theoretical wisdom (knowledge) and practical wisdom (action for benefit). He then distinguishes between acting for one's own benefit (motive of desire) and acting for another's benefit (motive of wisdom), clearing the ground for proving God's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Read the definition of wisdom
Read the Arabic passage where al-Razi defines wisdom with two senses. Note the two terms: theoretical wisdom and practical wisdom.
Theoretical wisdom is about knowledge; practical wisdom is about actions.
2
Understand the two motives
Al-Razi distinguishes between da'iyat al-shahwa (motive of desire) and da'iyat al-hikma (motive of wisdom). Focus on the difference: self-benefit vs. other-benefit.
For God, the motive of desire is impossible because He is free from desire.
3
Connect to God's attributes
Since God's knowledge is perfect, His wisdom is perfect. The passage shows that God's wisdom is not like human wisdom mixed with desire.
This sets up the need to prove the motive of wisdom for God.

Chapter 6: Proving that He, exalted, is Wise. Know that wisdom is explained by two things: one is theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of the realities of things. The knowledge of God, exalted, is the most complete and best of knowledges, so necessarily His wisdom is the best and most complete of the types of wisdom. The second is practical wisdom, which is an expression of the action that leads to obtaining benefit and repelling harm. Then we say: if the purpose of that action is to bring benefit to oneself and repel harm from oneself, then this is called the motive of desire. And if the purpose of that action is to bring benefit to another and repel harm from another, then this is called the motive of wisdom. Since it has been established by proofs that God, exalted, is free from desire and aversion, pain and pleasure, the motive of desire is impossible to be established in His right. The discussion remains regarding establishing the motive of wisdom for God, exalted. So we say: The philosophers have a way regarding this required matter, and the Mu'tazila have another way. We will mention each one in the best manner. As for the way of the philosophers: they said: reason judges that existence either necessitates that it be pure good, or it comprises good and evil but good is predominant, or they are equal, or good is overcome, or it is pure evil. They said: As for pure good, wisdom necessitates its bringing into existence and formation. As for that which comprises good and evil but good predominates over evil, wisdom also necessitates its formation, because abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil, and it cannot be said: Why did the Creator not create this part such that it would be free of that little evil? For we say: (The discussion is about the good which it is rationally impossible to separate from that little evil. This part is such that wisdom necessitates its formation, because obtaining that much good, independent of that little evil, since it is rationally impossible, nothing remains except one of two things: one is that we abandon that much good due to caution against that little evil, and we have shown that abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil; and the second is that we accept that little evil for the sake of desire to obtain that much good. So it is established that in this part there occurs a conflict between bearing much evil and bearing little evil, and clear reason declares that bearing little evil is preferable to bearing much evil. Thus it is established that the part whose good outweighs its evil is necessarily to be obtained in wisdom. As for the third part, that in which good and evil are equal, wisdom does not necessitate bringing it into existence. As for the fourth and fifth, it is clear that they are against wisdom. If this is established, we say: The creatures of God, exalted, are either pure goods, or if they contain evil, the good is predominant. For the hungry, though many, the satiated are more. The sick, though many, the healthy are more. And the defective... (1) addition (2) from (M). (3) from (M). (4) from (M). (5) fewer (S).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines wisdom and presents the philosophers' argument for God's wisdom. It opens by defining wisdom (hikma) in two senses: theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of realities, and practical wisdom, which is action that brings benefit and repels harm. God's knowledge is perfect, so His wisdom is perfect. The author then distinguishes between two motives: the motive of desire (da'iyat al-shahwa), which aims at one's own benefit, and the motive of wisdom (da'iyat al-hikma), which aims at benefiting others. Since God is free from desire and aversion, the motive of desire is impossible for Him, so we must prove the motive of wisdom for Him. The author declares that philosophers and Mu'tazila have different proofs; he will present the philosophers' proof. The philosophers argue that reason judges existence to fall into five categories: pure good, good with predominant good, equal good and evil, good overcome by evil, and pure evil. For pure good, wisdom necessitates its existence. For good with predominant evil, wisdom also necessitates it because abandoning much good for little evil is a greater evil; and if the little evil is inseparable from the good, reason prefers the lesser evil to preserve the greater good. For equal good and evil, wisdom does not necessitate it; for the latter two, they are against wisdom. The author then applies this to creatures: they are mostly good or have predominant good, e.g., the satiated outnumber the hungry, the healthy outnumber the sick, etc., though the text is cut off. The argument shows that the existing world, with its mixture of good and evil, is the product of wisdom because the good predominates.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think wisdom is only about knowledge, not action.
Correction: The passage defines wisdom as both theoretical (knowledge) and practical (action for benefit).

Checkpoint
According to al-Razi, what is the 'motive of wisdom' (da'iyat al-hikma)?
A) Acting to benefit oneself
B) Acting to benefit another
C) Acquiring knowledge
D) Avoiding harm to oneself

Key Takeaway
Wisdom for God means both perfect knowledge and acting for the benefit of others, free from any selfish desire.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you help others, do you ever pause to examine your true motive? Is there a hidden desire for praise, reciprocity, or self-image? This lesson forces you to ask: can you perform an act with the pure motive of wisdom (benefit for others alone) even once today?
?
Theoretical wisdom is knowing the realities of things. You claim to know that God is All-Wise. But does that knowledge penetrate your daily decisions? When something goes wrong, do you truly believe God's action is wise for you, or do you complain? What would it take for you to live in complete trust of His motive?
?
The passage teaches that God is free from desire. Consider your own desires: your cravings for comfort, approval, control. How often do they drive your actions? If you could act with God-like detachment from self-interest for one interaction today, what would it be? Commit to it now.

The Philosophers' Proof for God's Wisdom
Al-Razi presents the philosophers' five-category argument.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' rational argument for God's wisdom based on the five categories of good and evil and the principle that wisdom requires creating what has predominant good.

Core Idea
A world with more good than evil is the product of wisdom.

After defining wisdom, al-Razi presents the philosophers' proof that God is wise. They argue that existence falls into five categories concerning good and evil. Pure good and predominant good must be created by wisdom; equal good and evil is not required; and predominant evil or pure evil are against wisdom. Applying this to the world, we see that good predominates, proving God's wisdom.

Guided Path
1
Identify the five categories
Read the passage and list the five categories of existence according to the philosophers: pure good, good predominant, equal, evil predominant, pure evil.
The categories are based on the ratio of good to evil.
2
Understand the ruling for each category
The passage states the requirement of wisdom for each category. Focus on the argument for the second category: why wisdom necessitates creating it even with some evil.
The key point: abandoning much good for a little evil is a greater evil.
3
Apply to the world
Al-Razi applies the categories to God's creatures, arguing that most are good or have predominant good. See the examples of hungry vs. satiated, sick vs. healthy.
The text cuts off, but the logic is clear.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The argument concludes that the existing world, with its mixture, is the product of wisdom because good outweighs evil.
This refutes the objection that a wise God would not create evil.

Chapter 6: Proving that He, exalted, is Wise. Know that wisdom is explained by two things: one is theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of the realities of things. The knowledge of God, exalted, is the most complete and best of knowledges, so necessarily His wisdom is the best and most complete of the types of wisdom. The second is practical wisdom, which is an expression of the action that leads to obtaining benefit and repelling harm. Then we say: if the purpose of that action is to bring benefit to oneself and repel harm from oneself, then this is called the motive of desire. And if the purpose of that action is to bring benefit to another and repel harm from another, then this is called the motive of wisdom. Since it has been established by proofs that God, exalted, is free from desire and aversion, pain and pleasure, the motive of desire is impossible to be established in His right. The discussion remains regarding establishing the motive of wisdom for God, exalted. So we say: The philosophers have a way regarding this required matter, and the Mu'tazila have another way. We will mention each one in the best manner. As for the way of the philosophers: they said: reason judges that existence either necessitates that it be pure good, or it comprises good and evil but good is predominant, or they are equal, or good is overcome, or it is pure evil. They said: As for pure good, wisdom necessitates its bringing into existence and formation. As for that which comprises good and evil but good predominates over evil, wisdom also necessitates its formation, because abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil, and it cannot be said: Why did the Creator not create this part such that it would be free of that little evil? For we say: (The discussion is about the good which it is rationally impossible to separate from that little evil. This part is such that wisdom necessitates its formation, because obtaining that much good, independent of that little evil, since it is rationally impossible, nothing remains except one of two things: one is that we abandon that much good due to caution against that little evil, and we have shown that abandoning much good for the sake of a little evil is a great evil; and the second is that we accept that little evil for the sake of desire to obtain that much good. So it is established that in this part there occurs a conflict between bearing much evil and bearing little evil, and clear reason declares that bearing little evil is preferable to bearing much evil. Thus it is established that the part whose good outweighs its evil is necessarily to be obtained in wisdom. As for the third part, that in which good and evil are equal, wisdom does not necessitate bringing it into existence. As for the fourth and fifth, it is clear that they are against wisdom. If this is established, we say: The creatures of God, exalted, are either pure goods, or if they contain evil, the good is predominant. For the hungry, though many, the satiated are more. The sick, though many, the healthy are more. And the defective... (1) addition (2) from (M). (3) from (M). (4) from (M). (5) fewer (S).
Source pages p. 280

Commentary
This passage defines wisdom and presents the philosophers' argument for God's wisdom. It opens by defining wisdom (hikma) in two senses: theoretical wisdom, which is knowledge of realities, and practical wisdom, which is action that brings benefit and repels harm. God's knowledge is perfect, so His wisdom is perfect. The author then distinguishes between two motives: the motive of desire (da'iyat al-shahwa), which aims at one's own benefit, and the motive of wisdom (da'iyat al-hikma), which aims at benefiting others. Since God is free from desire and aversion, the motive of desire is impossible for Him, so we must prove the motive of wisdom for Him. The author declares that philosophers and Mu'tazila have different proofs; he will present the philosophers' proof. The philosophers argue that reason judges existence to fall into five categories: pure good, good with predominant good, equal good and evil, good overcome by evil, and pure evil. For pure good, wisdom necessitates its existence. For good with predominant evil, wisdom also necessitates it because abandoning much good for little evil is a greater evil; and if the little evil is inseparable from the good, reason prefers the lesser evil to preserve the greater good. For equal good and evil, wisdom does not necessitate it; for the latter two, they are against wisdom. The author then applies this to creatures: they are mostly good or have predominant good, e.g., the satiated outnumber the hungry, the healthy outnumber the sick, etc., though the text is cut off. The argument shows that the existing world, with its mixture of good and evil, is the product of wisdom because the good predominates.

Key Terms
خير محض
شر محض
تغليب الخير

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that a wise God would create a world with no evil at all.
Correction: The passage argues that wisdom permits evil if it is outweighed by good and inseparable from it.

Checkpoint
According to the philosophers' argument, what is the ruling for a part of existence where good and evil are equal?
A) Wisdom necessitates its existence.
B) Wisdom does not necessitate its existence.
C) Wisdom prohibits its existence.
D) Wisdom is indifferent.

Key Takeaway
The philosophers prove God's wisdom by showing that the world, with its predominant good, falls into the category that wisdom requires to create.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you experience a small evil or hardship in your life, do you immediately complain and focus on that evil, or do you look for the greater good that may be inseparable from it? How does this lesson challenge your natural impatience with trials?
?
Consider a situation where you had to choose between avoiding a minor discomfort and gaining a major benefit. Did you choose the benefit, or did you avoid the discomfort? What does that tell you about your own understanding of wisdom?
?
The philosophers argue that a wise Creator necessarily creates a world where good outweighs evil. Do you truly believe that the good in your own life outweighs the evil, even when you are in pain? If not, what does that say about your trust in God's wisdom?

Divine Attributes
intermediate

The Five Categories and the Necessity of Wisdom
Enumeration of five categories and argument for the first two.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five logical categories of good and evil and why divine wisdom necessitates the existence of pure good and predominantly good things, even when they involve unavoidable minor evils.

Core Idea
Wisdom requires creating what is good or mostly good; abandoning much good to avoid little evil is a greater evil.

Before we can appreciate the wisdom in creation, we need a clear classification of the possible mixtures of good and evil. The author enumerates five types and then argues that true wisdom demands the first two: pure good and good that outweighs evil. The argument involves a rational calculation where avoiding a small evil at the cost of a greater good is deemed irrational.

Guided Path
1
Read the classification of five types
Identify the five categories listed in the first sentence. Write them down: pure good, good predominant, equal, evil predominant, pure evil.
These categories are exhaustive: every possible state of good and evil falls into one.
2
Understand the argument for pure good
The author states that wisdom necessitates pure good. Why? Because there is no drawback, so it is purely beneficial. Ask yourself: would a wise creator withhold pure good?
3
Analyze the argument for predominant good
This is the core argument. The author says abandoning much good for little evil is itself a great evil. He then addresses the objection: why not create it without that little evil? The answer: it is rationally impossible to separate the good from that evil.
Notice the two options: either accept the little evil and get the great good, or avoid the evil and lose the great good. Reason chooses the first.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that the category with predominant good is necessary to attain according to wisdom. This establishes that wisdom does not require a world free of all evil, only that good outweighs evil when unavoidable.
This refutes the misconception that any evil disproves divine wisdom.

مرضي به ، فلأن المقصود من الخلق إنما هو تحصيل الخير ، وأما أن الشر مراد مكروه فإنه نظراً إلى ذاته من حيث إنه مكروه ، إلا أنه لكونه من لوازم ذلك الخير المطلوب بالذات ، صار مراداً فهو مكروه بالذات ، It necessitates that it be pure good, or that it be composed of good and evil but good is predominant, or that they be equal, or that good be overcome, or that it be pure evil. They said: As for pure good, wisdom necessitates its existence and formation. And as for that which is composed of good and evil but good predominates over evil, wisdom also necessitates its formation because abandoning much good for the sake of little evil is much evil. And it cannot be said: Why did the Creator not create this category such that it be free of that little evil? Because we say: the discussion concerns the good which is rationally impossible to be free from that little evil. This category is what wisdom necessitates its formation because attaining that much good while being free from that little evil, since it is rationally impossible, nothing remains except one of two things: one: that we abandon that much good for the sake of avoiding that little evil; and we have shown that abandoning much good for the sake of little evil is much evil. The second: that we accept that little evil for the sake of desiring to attain that much good. So it is established that in this category there is a conflict between bearing much evil and bearing little evil, and clear reason declares that bearing little evil is preferable to bearing much evil. Thus it is established that the category whose good outweighs its evil is necessary to attain according to wisdom. As for the third category, in which good and evil are equal, wisdom does not necessitate its existence. As for the fourth and fifth, it is clear that they are contrary to wisdom. If this is established, we say: The creatures of God Almighty are either pure goods, or if they contain evil, nevertheless good in them is predominant. For the hungry, though many, the full are more; the sick though many, the healthy are more; the defective though many, the sound are more. Thus it is established by what we have mentioned that all creatures of God Almighty occur in accordance with wisdom and correctness. And upon this, they said: Good is desired and pleasing, evil is desired and disliked. As for good being desired and pleasing, because the purpose of creation is only to attain good. As for evil being desired and disliked, it is with regard to its essence in that it is disliked, except that because it is a necessary concomitant of that good which is desired in essence, it becomes desired. So it is disliked in essence, desired by purpose and as a secondary matter. A wise poet versified this meaning, saying: Gray hair is disliked, and we dislike to part. I marvel at something loved despite hatred. The meaning is that gray hair causes deficiency in strength and life, so it is disliked in essence. But since life cannot occur except with its occurrence, and life is loved, it follows that gray hair is loved. This necessitates that gray hair is both loved and hated, but it is loved by purpose, hated in essence. This is the complete discussion in establishing this chapter.
Source pages p. 281

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of possible states of good and evil in creation and argues for divine wisdom. It begins by enumerating five types: (1) pure good, (2) good predominant over evil, (3) equal good and evil, (4) evil predominant, (5) pure evil. The author then argues that wisdom requires the existence of the first two types. For pure good, it is obvious. For the second type, where good is predominant, abandoning much good to avoid little evil is itself a greater evil, so wisdom necessitates creation despite the lesser evil. The author anticipates an objection: why not create it without that evil? He clarifies that this applies only when it is rationally impossible to separate the good from that evil. In that case, two options remain: abandon much good or accept little evil; reason dictates the latter. The third type (equal) is not required by wisdom, while the last two are contrary to wisdom. The author then applies this to all creatures, arguing that in the world, good predominates (more healthy than sick, etc.), so creation is wise. Finally, he explains the status of good and evil: good is intended for itself and pleasing; evil is intended only as a necessary means to a greater good, so it is disliked in essence but desired by purpose, illustrated by the example of gray hair which is disliked but accepted because life depends on it.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The presence of any evil in creation shows that the world is not created with wisdom.
Correction: The author shows that if the evil is unavoidable and outweighed by a greater good, then wisdom actually necessitates creating it, because avoiding it would mean losing a greater good, which is a greater evil.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason wisdom necessitates the existence of things where good is predominant over evil?
A) Because pure evil cannot exist without good
B) Because abandoning much good for little evil is itself a greater evil
C) Because the Creator cannot create anything with evil
D) Because good and evil are always equal in creation

Key Takeaway
Divine wisdom requires the existence of pure good and predominantly good things; a small unavoidable evil is permissible when it is the lesser of two evils.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Can you identify an area in your life where you have been avoiding a great good—like a difficult act of worship, a relationship mending, or a sacrifice—because of a small fear or discomfort? How does this lesson's principle of accepting a lesser evil for a greater good challenge you to act now?
?
Think of a time when you complained about a hardship or a small evil in your life. Did you consider that this hardship might be the unavoidable price for a much greater good you already enjoy? How can you shift your perspective to thankfulness for the outweighing good?
?
This lesson shows that wisdom sometimes requires choosing the lesser of two evils. What 'lesser evil' are you currently avoiding that, by choosing it, would unlock a much greater good for yourself or others? What is stopping you from making that choice today?

Creation as Wise and the Status of Good and Evil
Discussion of remaining categories, application to creation, and status of good and evil.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the remaining categories (equal, evil predominant, pure evil) are not required by wisdom, why the actual world is dominated by good, and how evil can be desired as a means to a greater good while being disliked in itself.

Core Idea
The world is mostly good; evil is tolerated only as a necessary means to a greater good.

Having established the necessity of the first two categories, the author now addresses the remaining three categories and applies the principle to actual creation. He argues that in the world, good overwhelmingly predominates over evil, so the universe is a product of wisdom. Finally, he clarifies the normative status of good and evil: good is desired for itself, while evil is desired only as a necessary accompaniment to good, like gray hair is disliked but accepted as part of life.

Guided Path
1
Examine the third, fourth, and fifth categories
Read the brief statements: equal good and evil is not required by wisdom; evil predominant and pure evil are contrary to wisdom. Understand that these are not found in creation according to the author.
The author does not prove they don't exist, but he will show that creation actually falls into the first two categories.
2
Observe the application to creatures
The author gives examples: more full than hungry, more healthy than sick, more sound than defective. This shows that good predominates. Therefore, all creatures occur with wisdom.
This is an inductive argument: the overall balance is positive.
3
Understand the status of good and evil
Good is desired in essence and pleasing. Evil is disliked in essence but desired by purpose when it is a necessary means to a greater good. The example of gray hair clarifies this: gray hair is disliked for itself but loved because it accompanies life.
This resolves the apparent contradiction: evil is both intended and hated, but on different levels.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Can you identify ways in which your own flaws or shortcomings are tolerated by God as means to a greater purpose? Are you willing to trust God's wisdom even when you cannot see the greater good?
