Objection: Are Need and Desire Harmful Creations?
Objection to the second proof with three sub-challenges.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the objector challenges the proof's premise that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful, and how each sub-objection pushes the argument deeper.

Core Idea
The objection questions whether need and desire are necessarily harmful and divinely created.

In this passage, an objector (fā'il qīla) raises three sequential challenges to the second proof's claim that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful. Each challenge forces a re-examination of the relationship between divine action, human attributes, and the meaning of grace. Understanding these objections is crucial for grasping the full kalam argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the repeated pattern: the objector concedes a point then asks a question.
The passage ends mid-example; focus on the argument's structure.
2
Identify the three sub-objections
The passage contains three distinct challenges: (1) why is need harmful? (2) even if harmful, why is it created by God? (3) even if created, why does that imply futility?
Each challenge begins with 'سلمنا' (we concede) followed by 'لم قلتم' (why did you say).
3
Analyze the first objection
The objector denies the conditional that grace requires need and desire, then asks: why is need harmful? What evidence is there?
This challenges the assumption that need is a harm.
4
Analyze the second objection
The objector concedes harm but questions whether it is created by God. He suggests need might be a necessary attribute of essence, like the evenness of four.
This pushes the discussion into the nature of attributes (lāzim al-dhāt).
5
Analyze the third objection and example
The objector concedes creation but argues that futility only follows if God gives exactly what is needed. If He gives more, it is not futile. The incomplete example illustrates this.
The example is cut off; consider how it would complete the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قيل : الشرطية ممنوعة وهي قوله : أن لا تكون النعمة نعمة ، إلا إذا كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إليها مشتهياً لها ، والحاجة والشهوة ضررهما من فعل الله - سبحانه - فتقول : لا نزاع في أنه ما لم يكن الحيوان محتاجاً إلى الشيء ومشتهياً له ، لم يكن إيصال ذلك الشيء إليه نعمة في حقه . لكن لم قلت : إن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة ضرر ؟ وما الدليل على أن الأمر كذلك ؟ سلمنا أنه ضرر ، ولكن لم قلتم : إن ذلك الضرر حاصل بتخليق الله تعالى ؟ وما الدليل عليه ؟ وتقريره : أن كون الإنان محتاجاً إلى الشيء وصف لازم لذاته ولماهيته ، ولما كان الأمر كذلك لم يكن حصوله بسبب جعل الجاهل وفعل الفاعل ، كما أن كون الأربعة زوجاً ، وكون الثلاثة فرداً ، ليس بسبب الجمل والخلق والتكوين . سلمنا أن الحاجة إلى الشيء والشهوة له ، إنما حصل بتخليق الله تعالى وإيجاده . فلم قلتم : إن على هذا التقدير يكون إيصال النعمة إليه عبثاً ؟ وتقريره : أن هذا العبث إنما يلزم إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى لا يوصل إليـه النعمة إلا بقدر حاجته وشهوته. أما إذا قلنا : إنه تعالى خلق الحاجة والشهوة فيه . ثم إنه بعد ذلك أعطاه من المنافع ما يزيد على قدر حاجته أضعافـاً مضاعفة ، فهذا يمنع من كونه عبثاً . ومثاله : أن من أوصل ديناراً من الغير إلى
If it is said: The conditional is denied, which is his statement: That grace is not grace unless the one graced is in need of it and desires it, and need and desire—their harm is from God—so we say: There is no dispute that as long as the animal is not in need of something and desiring it, conveying that thing to it is not a grace for him. But why did you say that need for something and desire are harmful? And what is the evidence that the matter is so? We concede that it is harmful, but why did you say that this harm comes about by God's creation? And what is the evidence for it? Its clarification: That a person's being in need of something is a necessary attribute of his essence and quiddity, and since the matter is so, its occurrence is not due to the act of an agent, just as four being even and three being odd is not due to making and creating. We concede that need for something and desire for it only come about by God's creation and bringing into existence. Then why did you say that on this estimation, conveying grace to him is futility? Its clarification: That this futility only follows if we say that He does not convey grace to him except to the extent of his need and desire. But if we say that He created need and desire in him, then afterwards He gave him benefits that exceed his need many times over, this prevents it from being futility. Its example: That one who conveys a dinar from another to...
Source pages p. 293

Commentary
This passage is an objection and the beginning of an answer to the second proof. The objector (fāʾil qīla) challenges a premise of the proof: the claim that grace requires prior need and that need is harmful. The objector first denies the conditional (al-sharṭiyya mamnūʿa) that grace only applies when the recipient is in need and desires the thing, and that need and desire are harmful actions of God. He then makes several points: (1) while it is agreed that without need and desire the gift is not a grace, why is need harmful? (2) Even if it is harmful, why is that harm created by God? Perhaps need is a necessary attribute of the essence (lāzim li-dhātihi) and not caused, like evenness of four. (3) Even conceding that God creates the need and desire, why does that imply futility? The futility only follows if God gives only enough to satisfy the need; but if He gives many times over what is needed, it is not futile. The objection ends with an incomplete example of a person giving a dinar.

Misconception: The assumption that need is always harmful and created by God.
Correction: The objector questions both: need might not be harmful, and even if harmful, it might not be created by God.

Checkpoint
What is one of the objector's points against the claim that need is harmful and created by God?
A) Need is actually beneficial.
B) Need may be a necessary attribute of essence, not caused.
C) God only creates need in sinners.
D) The proof is circular.

Key Takeaway
The objector exposes unresolved premises about the nature of need and divine causation within the proof.

Proportional Compensation for Prior Need
Razi argues that the amount of blessing equals the prior harm or need.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Razi's argument that the amount of divine blessing exactly equals the prior harm or need, and why this matters for divine justice.

Core Idea
Divine blessing perfectly compensates prior harm.

Razi presents a logical argument that the degree of pleasure or blessing we receive corresponds directly to the intensity of our prior desire or need. This is not merely psychological but theological: it shows that divine rewards are proportionate to prior hardship, ensuring perfect justice. Let's follow his reasoning step by step.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation to grasp the overall structure of the argument.
Notice that Razi uses a mathematical equality principle in his conclusion.
2
Identify the premises about causation
Find where Razi argues that disparities in desires are caused by God, not nature, and why this is essential for the discussion of good and evil.
He dismisses the necessitarian view as irrelevant to this inquiry.
3
Trace the correlation between desire and pleasure
Razi states that the stronger the desire, the more complete the pleasure upon fulfillment. Mark this step as the link between prior need and present blessing.
This is a psychological principle he treats as evident.
4
Understand the equality chain
Razi concludes that the amount of benefaction equals the amount of need/desire, which is harm. By transitivity, present blessing equals prior harm. See how he uses 'equals of equals' to finish the argument.
The argument is complete when this equality is established.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهذا التفاوت أمر معلوم بالضرورة، فاختصاص أحد ذينك الشخصين بتلك الرغبة العظيمة دون الثاني، إما أن يكون بالطبع أو بالخالق المختار. والأول بيناه. لأنا بينا في أول هذا الباب: أن الكلام في الحسن والقبح والحكمة والسفه فرع على القول بإثبات الفاعل المختار. وإما بتقدير أن يكون المؤثر في وجود العالم موجباً بالذات، يسقط هذا البحث بالكلية فيثبت: أن ذلك التفاوت لم يحصل إلا لأجل أن الله سبحانه خص أحدهما بتلك الرغبة والشهوة، وخص الثاني بتلك النفرة والكراهية. وكذا القول في الرغبة في أكل الأطعمة الشهية. فرب إنسان كانت رغبته في طعام معين، وكانت نفرته عن غير ذلك الطعام متأكدة، ورب إنسان بالضد من ذلك، فيثبت: أن هذه الشهوات المختلفة والرغبات المتضادة، إنما حصلت بإيجاد الله تعالى. إذا ثبت هذا فنقول: كلما كان الميل إلى الشيء والرغبة فيه والحرص عليه أشد، كان الالتذاذ بوجدانه أتم وأكمل، وكلما كان هذا المعنى أقل، كان الالتذاذ بوجدانه أضعف. وإذا ثبت هذا فنقول: إن مقدار الإنعام مساوي لمقدار اللغة، ومقدار اللذة مساوي لمقدار الحاجة السابقة ومقدار الشهوة السابقة. وقد دللنا على أن الحاجة والشهوة ضرر. والمساوي للمساوي للشيء مساوي لذلك الشيء. فيلزم أن يكون مقدار الإنعام الحاضر، مساوياً لمقدار الضرر السابق. وحينئذ يتم الكلام.
This disparity is a matter known by necessity. So the specification of one of those two persons with that great desire without the other is either by nature or by the free Creator. The first we have explained, for we explained at the beginning of this chapter that discourse on good and evil, wisdom and foolishness, is subsidiary to affirming the free agent. As for the assumption that the effecting influence in the existence of the world is necessary by essence, this entire inquiry falls away. Thus it is established that that disparity did not occur except because God—glorified be He—specified one of them with that desire and appetite, and specified the second with that aversion and repulsion. The same holds for the desire to eat palatable foods. A person may have a strong desire for a specific food and a strong aversion to other food, and another person may be the opposite. Thus it is established that these varied appetites and conflicting desires only occurred by God's bringing them into existence. When this is established, we say: the stronger the inclination to a thing, the desire for it, and the eagerness for it, the more perfect and complete is the pleasure upon finding it; and the weaker this meaning, the weaker the pleasure upon finding it. And when this is established, we say: the amount of benefaction equals the amount of [the prior] need; the amount of pleasure equals the amount of the prior need and the amount of the prior desire. And we have demonstrated that need and desire are harm. And what equals what equals a thing equals that thing. So it follows necessarily that the amount of present benefaction equals the amount of the prior harm. And at this point the discourse is complete.
Source pages p. 295

Commentary
This passage is an argument establishing that the amount of divine blessing equals the amount of prior harm or need. The author first argues that differences in desires and aversions among people are caused by God, the free Creator, not by nature, because the discussion of good and evil presupposes a free agent. If the world's cause were necessary, the whole inquiry would be irrelevant. He then notes that the strength of desire determines the strength of pleasure upon fulfillment. Since desire/need is a harm (something undesirable), and pleasure equals the measure of prior need, it follows that the blessing exactly compensates that prior harm. The argument uses a mathematical principle: if A = B and B = C, then A = C.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The argument denies that needs are harmful.
Correction: It affirms that the blessing compensates exactly, so no injustice; needs are indeed harmful, but the compensation is proportional.

Checkpoint

A) The object's intrinsic worth
B) The strength of the prior desire
C) The giver's generosity
D) The person's moral state

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that divine blessing is exactly proportionate to prior hardship, ensuring perfect justice.

How does this realization change the way you thank Allah for that blessing? Are you willing to acknowledge that your craving itself was a form of harm that He compensated?
?
Consider a current struggle or unmet desire. If Razi's argument is true, then the relief or attainment you seek will be exactly equal to your present hardship. Does that help you trust Allah's justice and timing? What practical step can you take today to surrender that desire to Him with full confidence in His compensation?
?
You have desires and aversions that you did not choose—they were assigned by God. Is there any desire or aversion you resent having? Can you accept it as part of Allah's plan for you, knowing that the pleasure of fulfilling it (or the relief from avoiding it) will perfectly match the prior difficulty? What would it look like to embrace your unique set of inclinations as a sign of His personal care?

Razi's Refutation of the Excess Objection
Razi refutes the objection that God could give more than needed.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Razi refutes the objection that God could give more than needed by distinguishing cases based on whether the recipient desires the excess.

Core Idea
An undesired excess cannot bring pleasure; a desired excess is just another need.

This passage addresses a subtle objection: if God causes need, could He not compensate by giving even more than needed? Razi's response is a tight logical dissection that distinguishes between cases where the recipient desires the extra and where they do not. The argument reveals an important principle: pleasure and benefit depend on desire, not just quantity.

Guided Path
1
Understand the objection
Read the objection carefully: why might someone think giving beyond need could be a net favor? The objector argues that despite God causing need, He can give extra to outweigh it.
2
Note the two-case analysis
Razi splits the problem into two possibilities: does the recipient have a desire (shahwa) for the excess, or not?
This is a classic logical division.
3
Follow the first case (desire present)
If the recipient desires the excess, then that excess is also a need. So the total favor equals the total harm (including the harm of the original need plus the new need for the excess). Thus the favor is not actually extra.
The 'favor' is exactly equal to the harm.
4
Follow the second case (no desire)
If there is no desire for the excess, then it cannot produce pleasure or benefit. Therefore, the excess is useless.
Pleasure comes from fulfilling desire.
5
Grasp the conclusion
In either case, giving beyond need does not result in a net favor greater than the harm. The objection fails.
The argument is complete and tight.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما قوله: لم لا يجوز أن يقال: إنه تعالى، وإن كان هو الذي أحوج، إلا أنه أعطى فوق قدر الحاجة؟ فالجواب: أن هذا باطل. لأن ذلك القدر الزائد، هل إليه شهوة وهل له في حصوله رغبة أو ليس كذلك؟ فإن كان الأول فحينئذ قد كان المنعم عليه محتاجاً إلى حصول تلك الزيادة كما كان محتاجاً إلى حصول ذلك الأصل، ولما كان محتاجاً إلى الأصل وإلى الزيادة معاً، فحينئذ يعود الأمر إلى أن النعمة إنما حصلت بقدر المضرة السالفة. وإن كان الثاني وهو أن لا يكون للمنعم عليه حاجة إلى وجدان تلك الزيادة، ولا يكون له شهوة إلى حصول تلك الزيادة، فنقول: الشيء الذي لا يكون محتاجاً إليه، ولا يكون بتعلق الشهوة والرغبة، امتنع أن يكون وجدانه سبباً لحصول اللذة والمنفعة.
As for his statement: 'Why is it not permissible to say that He—exalted is He—even though He is the One Who made [them] needy, yet He gave beyond the measure of need?' The answer: this is false. Because that excess amount—is there an appetite for it and a desire for its attainment, or not? If it is the first, then the one upon whom the favor is bestowed was in need of the attainment of that excess just as he was in need of the attainment of that original [need], and since he was in need of the original and of the excess together, then the matter returns to the fact that the favor only occurred in the measure of the prior harm. And if it is the second, i.e., that the one upon whom the favor is bestowed has no need for finding that excess, and has no appetite for the attainment of that excess, then we say: a thing that is not needed, and with which no appetite or desire is connected, it is impossible that its finding be a cause for the attainment of pleasure and benefit.
Source pages p. 296

Commentary
This passage is an answer to an objection. The objector suggests that God, though causing need, could give more than needed, making it a net blessing. The author refutes by dividing into two possibilities. First, if the recipient has a desire for the extra amount, then that extra is also a need, and the total favor equals the total previous harm (including the extra need). Second, if there is no desire for the extra, then it cannot produce pleasure or benefit, because benefit comes from fulfilling desire. Thus, giving beyond need is either not really extra (because it corresponds to an additional need) or it is useless. Therefore, the objection fails.

Correction: The author does not deny God's power; he argues that such excess would either be needed (thus not truly excess) or produce no pleasure.

C) The recipient will develop desire later

Key Takeaway
Razi's argument shows that a favor beyond need is either not truly beyond need (if desired) or does not produce pleasure (if not desired), so the objection fails.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

What does that reveal about your own understanding of what is truly beneficial? Will you dare to ask Allah to give you only what is best for you, even if it means not getting what you impulsively crave?
?
The objection assumes that giving more is always good, but Razi shows it can be pointless. How often do you measure God's favor by quantity rather than by genuine need? If you were to honestly assess your life, would you say that what you have is exactly what you need—or are you clinging to excesses that do not bring you closer to Him? Commit to listing three things you possess that you do not truly need, and reflect on whether they have increased your gratitude or your heedlessness.

The First Argument: Faith Would Change God's Knowledge
Al-Razi's first reductio argument about foreknowledge

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's first argument that if faith occurred despite God's foreknowledge of disbelief, it would imply God's knowledge becoming ignorance, which is impossible.

Core Idea
If faith occurs against God's knowledge, God's knowledge would become ignorance.

Al-Razi begins his demolition of the Mu'tazilite position on taklif with a simple logical argument. If God knows that someone will never believe, yet commands them to believe, then if they do believe, God's knowledge would no longer match reality—it would become ignorance. This is impossible, so the command is impossible.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Notice the conditional structure: 'if he believes... then...'.
The passage is very concise; each word carries weight.
2
Identify the key claim
Al-Razi argues that the occurrence of faith would necessitate a change in God's knowledge from true to false, which is impossible. Understand why this follows from the nature of knowledge and command.
God's knowledge is eternal and immutable; any change would be impossible.
3
Reflect on the conclusion
If the consequence (changing God's knowledge) is impossible, then the command that would lead to it is also impossible. This is a straightforward reductio ad absurdum.
This argument sets the foundation for the more complex faces that follow.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.
If he believes, then the occurrence of faith from him would necessitate the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, and what necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. So the obligation is an obligation of the impossible. This has been mentioned in a third way: that the existence of faith is impossible to occur together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, because it would only be knowledge if it corresponded to the known, and the knowledge of the absence of faith would only correspond if the absence of faith occurred. So if faith existed together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, the conjunction of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible. So commanding the acquisition of faith together with the existence of knowledge of the absence of faith is a command to combine two opposites, indeed a command to combine non-existence and existence, and all that is impossible. We mention this in a fourth way: that He, exalted, obligated those about whom He informed that they will never believe to believe, and faith requires believing God in all that He informed about, and from what He informed is that they will never believe. So they become obligated to believe and obligated to never believe, and this is an obligation combining negation and affirmation. We mention this in a fifth way: that He, exalted, blamed the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about, saying: 'They wish to change the words of God. Say: You will not follow us. Thus God has said before.' So it is established that intending to bring about what God has informed about its non-occurrence is an intention to change God's words, and that is what God has forbidden in this verse. Then in other verses God informed about them that they will never believe, so intending to attain faith becomes an intention to change God's words, and that is prohibited. Then He commanded everyone to attain faith, so this is a combination of command and prohibition regarding one thing, and that is an impossible obligation. Know that this discourse is the discourse that demolishes the foundations of Mu'tazilism. I have exerted myself to provide many words for them, and I mention them here exhaustively.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite (al-Razi's) argument that the Mu'tazilite position on taklif (obligation) is impossible if God has foreknowledge that certain people will never believe. It gives five formulations (wujuh) of the same essential argument. First, it argues that if faith occurs despite God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, that would turn God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible. Second (implied by the flow), it notes that knowledge must match reality; if God knows non-faith, then faith cannot occur without contradiction. Third, it explicitly states that the occurrence of faith with knowledge of non-faith would unite two contradictories. Fourth, it points out that God informed that some will never believe, so commanding them to believe requires them to both believe and not believe simultaneously—a contradiction. Fifth, it uses the Quranic verse about those who wish to change God's words: trying to achieve faith when God has said they won't is attempting to change God's words, which is forbidden. Furthermore, God commands all to believe, but has also informed that some will not, so this imposes a command and a prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation. Al-Razi concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism, and he has prepared extensive responses to their objections.

Key Terms
التكليف بالمحال
علم الله
استحالة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the first argument, what would the occurrence of faith (despite God's knowledge of non-faith) necessitate?
A) The increase of God's knowledge
B) The change of God's knowledge into ignorance
C) The affirmation of human free will
D) The nullification of the command

Key Takeaway
The first argument shows that if faith occurs despite God's foreknowledge, God's knowledge would become ignorance, which is impossible, so the command is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever excused your own spiritual laziness by telling yourself that if God had wanted you to be more faithful, He would have created you that way? How does al-Razi’s argument strip away that excuse? Write down one specific area where you will stop hiding behind divine decree and take responsibility tonight.
?
Imagine you are standing before God and He says: 'You knew My knowledge was perfect, yet you acted as if My knowledge could be overruled.' Does your daily life treat God’s knowledge as if it were negotiable? What would it look like to live with the awe that God already knows—and still holds you accountable? Commit to one action tomorrow that reflects this truth.
?
Al-Razi argues that commanding the impossible is absurd. Are there any areas in your life where you claim to obey God but secretly believe His commands are too difficult or unreasonable? If so, what does that say about your view of God's wisdom? Repent of one such thought right now and replace it with trust.

The Third and Fourth Arguments: Combining Contradictories
Third and fourth faces showing contradictions in taklif

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's third and fourth arguments showing that commanding belief in light of foreknowledge entails combining contradictories (existence and non-existence, affirmation and negation).

Core Idea
Commanding belief with foreknowledge of disbelief forces a contradiction.

Al-Razi presents two more refined formulations of the same problem. The third argument shows that the existence of faith cannot coexist with God's knowledge of its non-existence, as that would require a logical contradiction. The fourth argument highlights that God's own information about future disbelief makes the command to believe a command to both affirm and negate that information—an impossibility.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two arguments
The passage is divided into two clear parts: one introduced as 'third way' and one as 'fourth way'. Read each separately and note their distinct formulations.
The third argument is about the impossibility of faith existing with God's knowledge of its absence.
2
Analyze the third argument's logic
The third argument uses the correspondence theory of knowledge: knowledge is only knowledge if it matches reality. If God knows non-faith, then for that knowledge to be true, non-faith must occur. If faith occurs, God's knowledge would be false, and that would be a contradiction (al-jam' bayn al-naqiḍayn).
This is more subtle than the first argument; it focuses on the definition of knowledge.
3
Understand the fourth argument's novelty
The fourth argument adds the element of God's own information (khabar). Since God has informed that some will never believe, faith requires believing that information. But if one believes, they must also believe God's word that they will never believe, which is a contradiction. They are commanded to both affirm and negate the same thing.
This argument ties the logical impossibility to the nature of faith as tasdiq (affirmation).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.
If he believes, then the occurrence of faith from him would necessitate the change of God's knowledge into ignorance, and what necessitates the impossible is itself impossible. So the obligation is an obligation of the impossible. This has been mentioned in a third way: that the existence of faith is impossible to occur together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, because it would only be knowledge if it corresponded to the known, and the knowledge of the absence of faith would only correspond if the absence of faith occurred. So if faith existed together with the knowledge of the absence of faith, the conjunction of two contradictories would follow, which is impossible. So commanding the acquisition of faith together with the existence of knowledge of the absence of faith is a command to combine two opposites, indeed a command to combine non-existence and existence, and all that is impossible. We mention this in a fourth way: that He, exalted, obligated those about whom He informed that they will never believe to believe, and faith requires believing God in all that He informed about, and from what He informed is that they will never believe. So they become obligated to believe and obligated to never believe, and this is an obligation combining negation and affirmation. We mention this in a fifth way: that He, exalted, blamed the disbelievers for attempting to do something contrary to what God informed about, saying: 'They wish to change the words of God. Say: You will not follow us. Thus God has said before.' So it is established that intending to bring about what God has informed about its non-occurrence is an intention to change God's words, and that is what God has forbidden in this verse. Then in other verses God informed about them that they will never believe, so intending to attain faith becomes an intention to change God's words, and that is prohibited. Then He commanded everyone to attain faith, so this is a combination of command and prohibition regarding one thing, and that is an impossible obligation. Know that this discourse is the discourse that demolishes the foundations of Mu'tazilism. I have exerted myself to provide many words for them, and I mention them here exhaustively.
Source pages p. 307

Commentary
This passage presents the Ash'arite (al-Razi's) argument that the Mu'tazilite position on taklif (obligation) is impossible if God has foreknowledge that certain people will never believe. It gives five formulations (wujuh) of the same essential argument. First, it argues that if faith occurs despite God's knowledge of its non-occurrence, that would turn God's knowledge into ignorance, which is impossible. Second (implied by the flow), it notes that knowledge must match reality; if God knows non-faith, then faith cannot occur without contradiction. Third, it explicitly states that the occurrence of faith with knowledge of non-faith would unite two contradictories. Fourth, it points out that God informed that some will never believe, so commanding them to believe requires them to both believe and not believe simultaneously—a contradiction. Fifth, it uses the Quranic verse about those who wish to change God's words: trying to achieve faith when God has said they won't is attempting to change God's words, which is forbidden. Furthermore, God commands all to believe, but has also informed that some will not, so this imposes a command and a prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation. Al-Razi concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism, and he has prepared extensive responses to their objections.

النفي والإثبات
استحالة
العلم بعدم الإيمان

Checkpoint
What is the core problem in the third argument?
A) Faith cannot occur without God's help
B) The existence of faith with God's knowledge of its absence would combine contradictories
C) God's foreknowledge limits human freedom
D) The command is too burdensome

Key Takeaway
The third and fourth arguments show that commanding belief in light of divine foreknowledge necessarily involves combining logical opposites, which is impossible.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever commanded someone to do something while knowing they would not do it? How does that compare to Allah's knowledge and command? Does this argument change how you view His commands to those whom He knew would not believe?
?
When you read the fourth argument—that to believe they must affirm that they will never believe—does it not make you tremble? Could your own faith be built on affirming something that contradicts your own reality? How would you respond if Allah informed you that you will never believe?
?
This lesson exposes a logical impossibility in combining divine foreknowledge and human obligation. But what about your own inner contradictions? Are there commands you know you should obey, yet you live as if you have 'knowledge' that you will fail? How does the impossibility in the lesson mirror the impossibility of living in denial of Allah's commands?

The Fifth Argument: Quranic Evidence and Conclusion
Fifth face: Quranic argument and final demolition of Mu'tazilism

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Razi's fifth argument from Quranic verses about changing God's words, and his conclusion that this demolishes Mu'tazilism.

Core Idea
Trying to achieve what God said will not happen is trying to change God's words.

Al-Razi strengthens his case by invoking a Quranic verse where disbelievers are blamed for trying to change God's words. He argues that commanding faith in those whom God has declared will never believe would be commanding an attempt to change God's words, which is forbidden. Thus, this is an impossible obligation. He concludes that this argument destroys the foundations of Mu'tazilism.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and locate the Quranic verse
The passage quotes part of a verse from the Quran (Surah al-Fath? Actually it says 'يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل: لن تتبعونا. كذلكم قال الله من قبل'). Identify the verse and understand its original context.
The verse is about the Bedouins who wished to follow the Prophet but were told they would not.
2
Understand the argument from 'changing God's words'
Al-Razi argues that if God has informed that someone will never believe, then trying to make them believe is an attempt to change what God has said. Since changing God's words is forbidden, the command to achieve faith is a command to do something forbidden, which is impossible.
This adds a moral dimension: the command conflicts with another divine prohibition.
3
Grasp the final point about combining command and prohibition
Al-Razi notes that God has both commanded all to believe and informed that some will not believe. Thus, for those individuals, the command to believe coexists with the prohibition against changing God's words, resulting in a command and prohibition on the same act—an impossible obligation.
This final point ties together the logical and moral contradictions.

يؤمن ، كان صدور الإيمان منه : يستلزم انقلاب علم الله جهلا ، ومستلزم المحال محال ، فالتكليف تكليف بالمحال وقد تذكر هذا على وجه ثالث وهو أن جود الإيمان يستحيل أن يحصل مع العلم يعدم الإيمان ، لأنه إنما يكون علماً لو كان مطابقاً للمعلوم ، والعلم بعدم الإيمان إنما يكون مطابقاً ، لو حصل عدم الإيمان . فلو وجد الإيمان مع العلم بعدم الإيمان لزم الجمع بين النقيضين وهو محال . فالأمر بتحصيل الإيمان مع حصول العلم بعدم الإيمان : أمر بالجمع بين الضدين ، بل أمر بالجمع بين العدم والوجود ، وكل ذلك محال ، ونذكر هذا على وجه رابع وهو : أنه تعالى كلف هؤلاء الذين أخبر عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة بالإيمان، والإيمان معتبر فيه تصديق الله تعالى في كل ما أخبر عنه ومما أخبر به أنهم لا يؤمنون قط . فقد صاروا مكلفين بأن يؤمنوا ، وبأنهم لا يؤمنون قط، وهذا تكليف بالجمع بين النفي والإثبات . ونذكر هذا على وجه خامس وهو أنه تعالى عاب الكفار على أنهم حاولوا فعل شيء على خلاف ما أخبر الله عنه فقال : ( يريدون أن يبدلوا كلام الله قل : لن تتبعونا . كذلكم قال الله من قبل ) فيثبت : أن القصد إلى تكوين ما أخبر الله عن عدم تكوينه : قصد لتبديل كلام الله ، وذلك مما قد نهى الله عنه في هذه الآية . ثم إن في سائر الآيات أخير الله تعالى عنهم بأنهم لا يؤمنون البتة ، فالقصد إلى تحصيل الإيمان ، يكون قصداً إلى تبديل كلام الله ، وذلك منهي عنه . ثم إنه أمر الكل بتحصيل الإيمان فكان هذا جمعاً بين الأمر والنهي بالنسبة إلى الشيء الواحد ، وذلك تكليف ما لا يطاق . واعلم أن هذا الكلام هو الكلام الهادم لأصول الاعتزال. ولقد تكلفت لهم كلمات كثيرة ، وأنا أذكرها مهنا على سبيل الاستقصاء.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that al-Razi's argument denies human free will and makes God unjust
Correction: Al-Razi's argument is not about free will or justice; it is about logical coherence. He does not deny that God can do whatever He wills; rather, he shows that the Mu'tazilite position leads to contradictions.

D) It is a recommendation, not a command

Key Takeaway
The fifth argument uses the Quranic prohibition against changing God's words to show that commanding belief in those who will never believe combines command and prohibition, making it impossible.

?

The First Scriptural Argument: Qur'anic Verses Affirm No Prevention
The first scriptural argument: Qur'anic verses indicate no prevention.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'an's rhetorical questions refute the claim that divine foreknowledge prevents faith.

Core Idea
Qur'anic questions blaming disbelief prove that God does not prevent faith.

This lesson examines the first of three scriptural arguments against the objection that God's foreknowledge makes faith impossible. The author shows that the Qur'an is filled with verses that question why people do not believe, implying that there is no effective prevention. If God had truly prevented faith, such questioning would be morally problematic.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage for the first argument. Note the logical analogy and the list of verses.
Pay attention to the structure: claim, analogy, evidence, concluding quote.
2
Understand the logical analogy
Understand how the author uses the analogy of a jailer to show that God's questioning would be absurd if He prevented faith.
3
Examine the cited verses
Identify the multiple Qur'anic verses quoted. They all question why disbelief persists.
The verses include those addressed to Iblis, to Musa, and general rhetorical questions.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
The author concludes that these verses explicitly deny prevention, and if divine foreknowledge were a preventer, these verses would be false.
This is a reductio ad absurdum.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والكلام في هذا الباب على مقامين :
المقام الأول : قالوا : الدلائل السمعية والعقلية مطابقة على أن العلم بعدم الإيمان والإخبار عن عدم الإيمان لا يوجب كون الإيمان ممتنع الوجود .
أما الدلائل السمعية فمن وجوه :
الحجة الأولى : إن القرآن مملوء من الآيات الدالة على أنه لا مانع من الإيمان . قال تعالى : ( وما منع الناس أن يؤمنوا إذ جاءهم الهدى ) ؟ وهو إنكار بلفظ الاستفهام . ومعلوم أن رجلا لو حبس رجلاً آخر في بيت بحيث لا يمكنه الخروج عنه ، ثم يقول له : ما يمنعك من التصرف في حوائجي ؟ كان ذلك قبيحاً ، وكذا قوله تعالى : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا بالله ) ؟ وقوله ( لإبليس) : ( ما منعك أن تسجد ) ؟ وقوله ( عن قول ) موسى عليه السلام لأخيه : ( ما منعك إذ رأيتهم ضلوا ) وقوله : ( فما لهم لا يؤمنون ) ؟ ( فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين ) ؟ ( عفا الله عنك . لم أذنت لهم ) ؟ لم تحرم ما أحل الله لك ) ؟ قال الصاحب بن عباد ، في فصل له في هذا الباب : كيف يأمر بالإيمان وقد منعه عنه ، وينهاه عن الكفر وقد جبله عليه ؟ وكيف يصرفه عن الإيمان ثم يقول : أن يصرفون ) ويخلق فيهم الإفك ، ثم يقول : أن تؤفكون ) ؟ وأنشأ فيهم الكفر ، ثم يقول لم تكفرون ) ؟ وخلق فيهم لبس الحق بالباطل ثم يقول : لم تلبسون الحق بالباطل ) ؟ وصدهم من السبيل ثم يقول : لم تصدون عن سبيل الله ) ؟ وحال بينهم وبين الإيمان . فقال : ( وماذا عليهم لو آمنوا ) وذهب بهم عن الرشيد ، ثم قال : ( فأين تذهبون ) وأضلهم عن الدين حتى أعرضوا ، ثم قال : فما لهم عن التذكرة معرضين ) . وحاصل الكلام ههنا : أن هذه الآيات تصريحها يدل على عدم المنع ولو كان العلم بالعدم والإخبار عن العدم مانعاً ، لزم كذب هذه الآيات بأسرها .
الحجة الثانية : إن الله تعالى قال : (رسلا مبشرين ومنذرين ، لئلا يكون للناس على الله حجة بعد الرسل ) وقال : ( ولو أنا أهلكناهم بعذاب من قبله ، لقالوا : ربنا لولا أرسلت إلينا رسولاً ، فتتبع آياتك من قبل أن نذل ونخزى ) فلما بين الله تعالى أنه ما أبقى لهم عذراً إلا وقد أزاله عنهم ، فلو كان علمه بكفرهم ، وخبره عن كفرهم مانعاً لهم عن الإيمان ، لكان ذلك من أعظم الأعذار ، وأقوى الوجوه الدافعة عنهم العقاب . ولما لم يكن كذلك علمنا أنه غير مانع .
الحجة الثالثة : إنه تعالى حكى عن الكفار في ( حم السجدة . أنهم قالوا : ( قلوبنا في أكنة مما تدعونا إليه ، وفي آذاننا وقر ) وإنما ذكر الله تعالى هذه الحكاية عنهم ذماً لهم بسبب أنهم قالوا هذا القول ، ولو كان العلم بعدم الإيمان ، والخبر عن عدم الإيمان ، مانعاً لهم عن الإيمان ، لكانوا صادقين في
And the discussion in this chapter is in two stations:
The first station: They said: The scriptural and rational proofs are congruent that knowledge of the absence of faith and the report of the absence of faith do not necessitate that faith be impossible of existence.
As for the scriptural proofs, they are in several aspects:
The first argument: Indeed the Qur'an is filled with verses indicating that there is no preventer of faith. He said, Most High: 'And what prevented the people from believing when guidance came to them?' and it is a denial by way of interrogation. And it is known that if a man imprisoned another man in a house such that he could not leave it, then he said to him: What prevents you from attending to my needs? that would be ugly. Similarly His saying, Most High: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed in Allah?' and His saying (to Iblis): 'What prevented you from prostrating?' and His saying (concerning the saying) of Musa, peace be upon him, to his brother: 'What prevented you when you saw them go astray?' and His saying: 'So what is [wrong] with them that they do not believe?' and 'So what is [wrong] with them that they turn away from the reminder?' and 'May Allah pardon you; why did you give them permission?' and 'Why do you prohibit what Allah has made lawful to you?' The Sahib bin Abbad said, in a section of his on this chapter: How can He command faith while He has prevented him from it, and forbid disbelief while He has compelled him to it? And how can He turn him away from faith then say: 'You are turned away?' and create in them falsehood then say: 'You are deluded?' and originate in them disbelief then say: 'Why do you disbelieve?' and create in them the mixing of truth with falsehood then say: 'Why do you mix truth with falsehood?' and hinder them from the path then say: 'Why do you hinder from the way of Allah?' and interpose between them and faith, then say: 'And what harm would it do them if they believed?' and take them away from the right guidance, then say: 'So where are you going?' and lead them astray from the religion until they turn away, then say: 'So what is [wrong] with them that they turn away from the reminder?' And the upshot of the discussion here is that these verses explicitly indicate the absence of prevention, and if knowledge of non-existence and the report of non-existence were a preventer, it would necessitate the falsity of all these verses.
The second argument: Indeed Allah, Most High, said: 'Messengers as bearers of good tidings and warners, so that mankind will have no argument against Allah after the messengers' and said: 'And if We had destroyed them with a punishment before it, they would have said: Our Lord, why did You not send to us a messenger so that we might have followed Your verses before we were humiliated and disgraced?' So when Allah, Most High, made clear that He did not leave any excuse for them except that He removed it from them, then if His knowledge of their disbelief and His report of their disbelief were a preventer for them from faith, that would be among the greatest of excuses and the strongest of aspects repelling punishment from them. And since it is not so, we know that it is not a preventer.
The third argument: Indeed He, Most High, related from the disbelievers in Ha Mim As-Sajdah that they said: 'Our hearts are in coverings from that to which you invite us, and in our ears is deafness.' And Allah, Most High, only mentioned this relation from them as a blame of them because they said this statement. And if knowledge of the absence of faith and the report of the absence of faith were a preventer for them from faith, they would be truthful in
Source pages p. 308

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the objection that God's foreknowledge and announcement that a person will not believe makes faith impossible. The author identifies this as the first of two stations in the chapter. The objectors claim that both scriptural and rational proofs indicate that divine foreknowledge does not render faith impossible; here the author presents the scriptural proofs. The first argument is that the Qur'an is filled with verses that condemn disbelief and question why people do not believe, implying that there is no effective prevention. If God had truly prevented them, such questioning would be morally repugnant, like a jailer asking his prisoner why he does not perform tasks. The author cites multiple Qur'anic verses where God interrogates about lack of belief or action, including verses addressed to Iblis, to Musa about his brother, and general rhetorical questions. He then quotes a statement from al-Sahib bin Abbad elaborating on the contradiction: if God prevents faith, how can He command it and blame for its absence? Each question in the Qur'an would be incongruous if divine prevention existed. The conclusion is that these verses explicitly affirm the absence of prevention, and if God's knowledge of disbelief were a preventer, all these verses would be false—which is impossible. The second argument appeals to the purpose of sending messengers: to remove all excuses. If divine foreknowledge were a preventer, it would be the strongest excuse for disbelief, but God has not allowed such an excuse; therefore it cannot be a preventer. The third argument (incomplete in this window) presents a verse where disbelievers claim their hearts are covered and ears deaf, and God condemns them for this claim. If divine foreknowledge prevented belief, their claim would be truthful, but God blames them, showing that their claim is false and that they are responsible for their unbelief. The passage clearly defends human responsibility while affirming God's foreknowledge.

Key Terms
المنع
العلم بعدم الإيمان
الإخبار عن عدم الإيمان
الآيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: God's foreknowledge of disbelief forces individuals to disbelieve, so they are not responsible.
Correction: The Qur'anic questioning shows that disbelief is blameworthy and that God does not prevent it; individuals have free will and are accountable.

Checkpoint
What logical analogy does the author use to argue that God's questioning of disbelief would be unjust if He prevented faith?
A) A teacher asking a student why he didn't study.
B) A jailer imprisoning a person and then asking why he doesn't attend to his needs.
C) A shepherd asking his sheep why they stray.
D) A king asking his subject why he rebels.

Key Takeaway
The Qur'an's rhetorical questions about disbelief are incompatible with the claim that God prevents faith, proving that divine foreknowledge does not necessitate disbelief.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the verse 'So what is wrong with them that they do not believe?' do you feel the weight of accountability? If you have been turning away from guidance, realize that Allah questions your delay. What is preventing you from immediate obedience? Write down one action you will take today to respond to His call.
?
The passage shows that it would be unjust for God to command faith while preventing it. Yet we often blame our sins on 'fate'. Are you using qadar as an excuse for your own negligence? Confess one area where you have mistakenly attributed your failure to Allah's decree, and resolve to take personal responsibility.
?
The jailer analogy exposes how hypocritical it is to blame another for what you yourself have caused. How often do you point fingers at your environment, upbringing, or circumstances for your lack of faith? Identify one specific excuse you have relied on, and commit to discarding it today, trusting that Allah's command is genuine and within your ability.
